CONTRIBUTION OF THE JĪYARS OF ŚRĪ AHOBILA MAṬHA TO ŚRĪVAIṢṆAVA PHILOSOPHY AND RELIGION

THESIS SUBMITTED TO THE UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS FOR THE AWARD OF THE DEGREE OF DOCTOR OF PHILOSOPHY IN VAISHNAVISM

RESEARCH SCHOLAR: MRS. K. ANURADHA, M.A. Department of Vaishnavism University of Madras Madras - 600 005

RESEARCH SUPERVISOR: DR. M. NARASIMHACHARY Professor & Head Department of Vaishnavism University of Madras. Madras - 600 005

DEPARTMENT OF VAISHNAVISM UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS MADRAS 600 005

JANUARY 1997 []

Preface

The Ahobila Maṭha is a Śrīvaiṣṇava centre of great fame and antiquity. It will be 600 years old in 1998. It derives the greatness from the presiding deity of the Maṭha, Śrī Mālola Nṛsiṁha, one of the nine Narasiṁhas atop the Ahobilam Hill, forming part of the Nallamalai ranges of Kurnool District in Andhra Pradesh. It is one of the 108 divyadeśas, sacred to Śrīvaiṣṇavas. The Āḻvārs have sung the glory of this holy place in their Divyaprabandha. The place has gained sanctity, because according to the scriptures (Brahmāṇḍa Purāṇa) this is the very place where Lord Narasiṁha manifested himself and killed the demon King Hiraṇyakaśipu.

During the six centuries of its existence, since its inception in 1398 A.D. when Kiḍāmbi Śrīnivāsācārya of Tirunārāyaṇapuram, a young man hardly twenty years of age, was inspired by Śrī Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha to renounce the world for propagating Rāmānuja Darśana to the present day, the Ahobila Maṭha has been headed by eminent scholars, inspired poets, intrepid builders and noted dramatists. The present pontifical head Śrīman Nārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika is 45th in the line. Carrying the mantle of his forbears in propagating the Śrīvaiṣṇava religion and philosophy handed down by the God-intoxicated Āḻvārs and the benign Ācāryas, His Holiness demonstrates how spirituality and [] monasticism can be effectively practised in a secular society. The propagation of Rāmānuja Darśana must go hand in hand with projecting the arcā worship of the Lord and the other great Ācāryas for encompassing the devotees into its fold. It is in this background that the contribution of the Jīyars of the Ahobila Maṭha gains in significance and has been chosen as the subject of the present Ph.D. programme.

The thesis is divided into five chapters. The first chapter is introductory in nature and deals with Śrīvaiṣṇavism as a religion and the basic tenets of the Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy - the inseparability of Śrī and Viṣṇu, the indispensability of an Ācārya for human redemption who acts as a mediator between the suffering soul and the compassionate Lord and the greatness and glory of Prapatti Yoga superseding Karma, Jñāna and Bhakti Yogas. The Vaḍakalai (Northern sect of Śrīvaiṣṇavism) ācārya paramparā originating from Lord Nārāyaṇa upto Śrīmad Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika, founder Jīyar of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha is also given in this chapter.

In the second chapter the Saṁnyāsa tradition is taken up for study. The duties enjoined in the saṁnyāsa āśrama according to Yādavaprakāśa’s Yatidharmasamuccaya and the Yatiliṅgasamarthana of Śrī Vatsya Varadācārya have been presented. The Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetics fall into four groups - kuṭīcaka, [] bahūdaka, haṁsa and paramahaṁsa- each latter one being superior to the previous one. The yajñopavīta, tridaṇḍa, śikhā, pātra and kaupīna are the five essential things which a Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetic should always possess. He has to perform the annual cāturmāsya vrata, starting with the saṅkalpa on the āṣāḍha pūrṇimā day, which is termed as saṅkalpa mahotsava.

The third chapter is biographical in nature, dealing with the lives of the Jīyars of Ahobila Maṭha, their sojourns to the holy shrines, some of which were inaccessible, traversing the length and breadth of India even in days of yore when there were no proper means of transport, their expositions of the grantha catuṣṭaya, construction of temples, digging of tanks, establishment of agrahāras, installation of the idols of pūrvācāryas in temples, the construction of Bṛndāvanas for them and establishment of Vedapāṭhaśālās. Of special significance in recent times, is the construction of the 13-tiered Rājagopuram at Śrīraṅgam costing 15 million rupees, by the 44th Jīyar, when he was 89 years old. This is the tallest tower in Asia, 165 ft. in length and 150 ft. in breadth and rising upto 226 ft. in height. The present 45th Jīyar is carrying on this holy tradition by expanding the activities of Maṭha, making the forest shrines of Ahobilam accessible to all by laying roads, providing rest houses and renovating the tanks and temples at Ahobilam and providing the pleasure of having darśana of the mūla and utsava [] idols of Mālola Nṛsiṁha in unison at Ahobilam, which had not taken place for many bygone years.

The fourth chapter is devoted to the study of the compositions of the Jīyars. These can be broadly classified into philosophical, religious and literary works though there is an undercurrent of an admixture of philosophy, religion and literature in all their works. It should be mentioned here that not all the Jīyars have contributed in this direction. The works are selected on the basis of their literary and philosophical importance. Smaller stotras like daśakas, aṣṭottaraśatas and maṅgalāśāsanas are not taken into consideration. Some of the works are highly scholarly disputations, some are elucidatory of important concepts like Puruṣakāra and Rahasyas, while some others are commentarial, expounding basic texts like the Śrībhāṣya and Gītārthasaṅgraha. As many as 25 works of the Pontiffs have been studied in this chapter. Some of the works are in the form of palm-leaf / paper manuscripts while a majority of them is available in print. The fact that the Pontiffs could find time to compose excellent treatises inspite of their busy schedule of ritual worship of the presiding deity Śrī Mālola Nṛsiṁha, temple renovations, periodical tours and so on, makes the study all the more inspiring and interesting.

The Jīyar of Iñjimeḍu, Śrīraṅga Śaṭhakopa, the 42nd Pontiff has also left his mark in the annals of the Maṭha by establishing Vedapāṭhaśālā, Prabhandha [] pāṭhaśālā, a Sanskrit College at Madurāntakam, bringing out the monthly Publication of the Maṭha, Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā, and conducting the annual Mālola Vidvatsadas where scholars in Vedānta were called upon to air their views. This is a tradition that is still continuing to the present day and is eagerly looked forward to, by the devout Public. The present Jīyar has written a Tamil commentary on the Viṣṇupurāṇa. In addition, His Holiness has edited the Śrīvaiṣṇava-Sadācāra-Nirṇaya of Śrī Raṅganātha Yatīndra, the 26th pontiff, and the Prapannajayantī-nirṇaya of the 27th Jīyar, Śrī Vīrarāghava Yatīndra Mahādeśika. He is also the editor of the Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā wherein his editorial is read with avid interest by layman and scholar alike.

In conclusion, the fifth chapter estimates and sums up the mighty work of the holy saints who are spreading the message of Rāmānuja, carrying the idol of Lord Mālola in their tours for the sake of rejuvenating the masses, performing pañcasaṁskāras and bharanyāsa to the devotees, encompassing them into the Śrīvaiṣṇava fold and spreading the messages of unity and synthesis not only in the Śrīvaiṣṇava community but among those belonging to other faiths. The Jīyars not only transmit the traditional truths through such activities as publishing Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā in different languages to cater to the needs of the people living in different states, and by giving lectures/discourses, and guiding and encouraging [] scholarly assemblies, but also by training the young and devout disciples in the traditional lore and grooming them to be future ācāryas and authentic interpreters of the glorious siddhānta of Śrī Rāmānuja. By leading an ascetic life and expounding simple truths, they lend a new meaning to life and offer solutions for the suffering humanity and thus exude an air of harmony and peace wherever they go.

It is now my pleasant duty to acknowledge gratefully the help and cooperation I received directly and indirectly from individuals and institutions in preparing this thesis. At the outset I must express my thanks to the authorities of the University of Madras for giving me the permission to register myself as a Ph.D. research scholar with the Department of Vaishnavism in January 1992. I am also grateful to them for granting me the University Research Fellowship from January 1995 till date.

I record with deep gratitude, the help and encouragement given to me by my Research Supervisior, Dr. M. Narasimhachary, Professor and Head of the Department of Vaishnavism, University of Madras. He has liberally spared his time and energy for me, though he has been unsparing in his demand for excellence in my method and presentation. He has been a source of constant guidance and inspiration to me in the course of my research work. The study of [] the scholarly works of the Jīyars was made enjoyable when my Guru explained the subtleties in their interpretation of various texts and cleared my doubts and questions very meticulously. But for his kind and timely help, I would not have been able to complete this task.

I extend my thanks to Dr. V.K.S.N. Raghavan, Professor, Department of Vaishnavism, University of Madras, for the personal interest he took in my academic progress. I am also thankful to Dr. M.A. Venkatakrishnan, Reader, Department of Vaishnavism, University of Madras, who has been helpful in completing my thesis. I would also like to thank Sri Ranganathan, Vaishnavism Department, University of Madras, for his kind help.

I must express my thanks to the Curator and staff, Government Oriental Manuscript Library, Madras for permitting me to use the library and provide necessary references from Catalogues and Journals. I would like to thank Mrs. Rajalakshmi in particular for helping me in my reference work there. My grateful thanks are also due to the Director, Adyar Library and Research Centre, Madras, and to the Director of the Radhakrishnan Institute for Advanced study in Philosophy, University of Madras, for permitting me to use their libraries in connection with my research work. [] I record with gratitude, the timely help rendered by Mrs. Kasthuri Raghavan and Master Mukundan, in typing the thesis neatly and accurately.

I will be failing in my duty if I do not record my deep sense of gratitude to my family members for the constant encouragement and help given to me with perfect understanding.

Last but not the least, I wish to record my respectful homage to His Holiness the 45th Jīyar of Ahobila Maṭha, who in his pūrvāśrama, blessed me and suggested this subject for my research work.

K. Anuradha

The thesis represents independent work on the part of the candidate prepared under my general supervision.

/Dr. M. Narasimhachary/ SUPERVISOR []

INTRODUCTION

Śrīvaiṣṇavism

The diversity of India is tremendous; it is obvious; it lies on the surface and anybody can see it . . . But disruptive tendencies gave rise immediately to an attempt to find a synthesis. Some kind of a dream of unity has occupied the mind of India since the dawn of civilization. That unity was not conceived as something imposed from outside . . . It was something deeper and within its fold, the widest tolerance of beliefs and customs was practised and every variety acknowledged and even encouraged.1

This extract from the letter written by Jawaharlal Nehru during his confinement in Ahmednagar Fort in 1940s, epitomizes the greatness of Bhāratavarṣa, deriving its strength from the unison of variegated cultures and lending it a special charm and uniqueness in the panorama of world thought. This strain of unity in diversity is the life-blood of Hinduism in general and Śrīvaiṣṇavism in particular. A detailed study of Śrīvaiṣṇava philosophy will reveal that it is this special characteristic that places Śrīvaiṣṇava system of religious philosophy, head and shoulders above any other system of thought. This fusion without division of apparently diverse elements is the very foundation of Śrī Rāmānuja Darśana i.e. the philosophy expounded by the great Śrīvaiṣṇava Ācārya, Śrī Rāmānuja.

अखिलभुवनजन्मस्थेमभङ्गादिलिेले39

It is rather interesting to note that the term Viśiṣṭādvaita came to be applied to this system only after Rāmānuja (1017-1137 A.D.). In fact, Rāmānuja has used the word “Viśiṣṭādvyayaykya”. It was Sudarśanasūri (early part of 13th century) author of the celebrated commentary, the Śrutaprakāśikā on Rāmānuja’s Śrībhāṣya (a commentary on Bādarāyaṇa’s Brahmasūtras) who used “Viśiṣṭādvaita” for the first time.40 The term literally means “Qualified Non-dualism”. It is in fact the Doctrine special of Pan-organismal Monotheism. Śrī Vedāntadeśika (i.e. Veṅkaṭanātha 1268-1369 A.D.) the illustrious follower of Rāmānuja explains this term in his Nyāyasiddhāñjana thus:

अशेष-चिद्-अचित्-प्रकारं ब्रह्म एकमेव तत्त्वम्। तत्र प्रकार-प्रकारिणोः प्रकाराणाञ्च मिथः अत्यन्तभेदेऽपि विशिष्ट-एक्यादिविवक्षया एकत्वव्यपदेशः।41

All are but parts of one stupendous whole Whose body Nature is, and God, the soul.42

So, philosophical knowledge consists in clearly understanding the nature of the three entities, cit, acit, and Īśvara, their distinctness from and also their relation to one another. These constitute the tattvatraya or the three Realities of Viśiṣṭādvaita. The knowledge of these entities is only a preliminary requisite to leading a life in such a way that the main purpose, specially of human life (viz. liberation from the cycle of birth and death), is achieved. The description of the means for achieving this end is termed as hita (viz.- jñāna, karma, bhakti and prapatti). The final goal “the consummation devoutly to be wished”,43 namely mokṣa is paramapuruṣārtha.

सत्यं वद, धर्मं चर, स्वाध्यायान्मा प्रमदः।44

etc; and, as a philosophy, Viśiṣṭādvaita is as complex and capable of generating a wide variety of prismatic interpretations as is evident from the very first aphorism of the Brahma-sūtras, athāto brahmajijñāsā. But the speciality of this Viśiṣṭādvaitic Śrīvaiṣṇavism lies in the stress laid on bhakti or loving devotion to the Lord and prapatti or the doctrine of voluntary self-surrender to the Lord’s Will realising fully well that —

दासभाताः स्वतः सर्वे ह्यात्मानः परमात्मनः।45 (All selves, by nature, are subservient to the Lord)

The body is bestowed upon man (dattam śarīram) by God, out of His immense compasssion (paramakāruṇya) so that man may reach God in the end. This body in fact is a boat (plava), a means for crossing the ocean of worldly life (saṁsārasāgara-nistaraṇa). The goal viz. sāyujyapadavī is distant, and the means strenuous - through exercise (abhyāsa) and detachment (vairāgya). The individual has dāsvatāntrya i.e. independence bestowed on him to choose the right way leading to God. The yearning to reach God may germinate in a man in one life or a series of lives. By his own misdeeds he

यस्यानुग्रहमिच्छामि धनं तस्य हराम्यहम्। बान्धवैश्च वियोगेन मूशं भवति दुःखितः॥ तेन दुःखेन सन्तप्तो यदि मां न परित्यजेत्। तं प्रसादं करिष्यामि यत्सुरैरपि दुर्लभम्॥46

“Whomsoever I wish to make the recipient of My grace, I deprive him of his wealth and wean him away from his near and dear ones. If even in this sorrowful state, he does not abandon me, I will shower on him such a grace that even the gods would envy.”

The operation of God’s grace to a mumukṣu who through his devotion surrenders to the Lord’s Will helplessly and with mahāviśvāsa through a spiritual guru, is inevitable. As is well known, aprārthito na gopāyet?47 (“protect not unless prayed for”).

पापिष्टः क्षत्रबन्धुश्च पुण्डरीकश्च पुण्यकृत्। आचार्यवत्तया मुक्तौ तस्मादाचार्यवान् भवेत्॥48

In fact the Ācārya is equated to the Lord in status —

यथा देवे तथा गुरौ।49 देवमिवाचार्यमुपासीत।50

Madhurakavi Āḻvār states ten points51 of similarity between the Ācārya and the Lord. Hence it is evident that before one obtains Bhagavatsambandha, Ācāryasambandha is an absolute necessity. An Ācārya is one who grants sadvidyā and is ajñānadhvāntarodhaka. Compare the Śrutivākya –

"आचार्यवान् पुरुषो वेद"52

So ācāryavattā is mokṣa-kāraṇa or better still, mokṣa-sādhana. For a mumukṣu it is essential to observe the Guruparamparā up to the Lord. It is “Abhagavattaḥ” i.e., originating from the Lord and not barring the Divine ones. According to the Śrutivākya –

"स चाऽऽचार्यवंशो ह्येघोऽसावसाविति आभगवतः"53

In the Śrīvaiṣṇava sampradāya the first guru is Lord Nārāyaṇa Himself—

तमिमं सर्वसंप्रमाचार्यं पितरं गुरुम्।54 अखिललोकानां गुरुर्नारायणो गुरुः।55 गुरुरसि गतिश्चासि जगताम्।56 शिष्यापिताः शरण्यमिति मुमुक्षवस्त्वाम् आद्यं गुरुं गुरुमरप्याधिगम्य॥57

Next only to Lord Nārāyaṇa and out of Her own will comes Śrī (personification of dayā) tempering the Lord’s justice through Her divine mercy. This aspect of forgiveness and compassion lends a new dimension to Śrīvaiṣṇava religion as it were. It is a religion of hope, positive in its

The quality of mercy is not strained; It droppeth as the gentle rain from heaven Upon the place beneath. It is twice blest: It blesseth him that gives and him that takes. ‘Tis mightiest in the mightiest; it becomes The throned monarch better than his crown, His sceptre shows the force of temporal power, The attribute to awe and majesty, Wherein doth sit the dread and fear of things. But mercy is above this sceptred sway. It is enthroned in the heart of kings. It is an attribute to God Himself And earthly power doth then show liketh God’s When mercy seasons justice.

Śrī is the divine Mediator called puruṣakāra and is co-eternal with and inseparable from the Lord (apṛthaksiddha) pleading for love and forgiveness. So justice and mercy are eternally blended in Him – in the Godhead Śrīman Nārāyaṇa.

Next in line comes Viṣvaksena. He is the controller of Vaikuṇṭha or Paramapada. According to tradition, he taught Nammāḻvār or Śaṭhakopa the philosophy of Śrīvaiṣṇava system.

The Nālāyira-divyaprabandham is the name by which the hymns of all the Āḻvārs came to be collectively called. The Āḻvārs were twelve in number and include a woman, the celebrated Godā or Āṇḍāḷ, to whom a magnificent temple was built in later years at Śrīvilliputtūr, her birth place.

Here, we can contain ourselves to mentioning about Nammāḻvār, the greatest of Śrīvaiṣṇava saints known by different names as Māṟan, Parāṅkuśa, Śaṭhakopa, Vakuḷābharaṇa and so on. He gave to the world four grand hymns – the Tiruviruttam, the Tiruvāśiriyam, the Periya Tiruvantādi, and the Tiruvāymoḻi. The Śrīvaiṣṇavas call them the four Vedas in Tamil garb, and Vedāntadeśika refers to the Tiruvāymoḻi as the Dramiḍopaniṣad. Nammāḻvār’s hymns have shaped the conduct and faith of the Vaiṣṇavas of South India. To the emotionally inclined these are simple appealing prayers to the different [] local arcā-avatāras of Lord Viṣṇu. To the less emotional and more philosophical, they seem to be the outcome of great learning in the Vedas, Upaniṣads, Gītā etc. To the practical spiritualist, they are of value as the achievement of one who was in holy communion with God. Nammāḻvār is called the “progenitor of the surrendered souls” (prapannajana santāna kūṭastha) by later Ācāryas of Viśiṣṭādvaita, and the fundamental doctrines of the Śrīvaiṣṇava faith, as current today, were taught by him.

After the age of the Āḻvārs (850 A.D.) we enter into a new epoch in the history of Śrīvaiṣṇavism - the age of Ācāryas. The Ācāryas expounded their system of thought based on the hymns of Āḻvārs and the ancient Sanskrit scriptures. Their object was to reconcile the Vedas, Upaniṣads and Gītā with the Tamil Prabandhams. In fact they aimed at interpreting the latter in terms of the former. In appreciation, posterity has conferred on them (the Ācāryas), the significant title of Ubhayavedāntins. They regarded the Prabandham as equally authoritative as the Vedas and introduced them in public and private worship. They widened the scope of spiritual studies so as to include Tamil scriptures. Further they elaborated and perfected the Viśiṣṭādvaita school of thought with the aid of the Doctrine of Self-surrender which they derived from the Āḻvārs. The Ācāryas were very orthodox, well-versed in Sanskrit and Tamil. They passed through the different stages of orthodoxy and discharged their nitya (daily) and naimittika (occasional) duties expected of an [] individual (even as the Lord Himself did in His avatāras) so that they may serve as standing examples to their followers. All the Śrīvaiṣṇava festivals, observances, fasts, vows and customs can be traced to the rules laid down by the Ācāryas. They were thus the makers of modern Śrīvaiṣṇavism - its society, rituals, practices and ideals. It is not therefore surprising that they, in turn, have become objects of worship and have been raised to equality with the Āḻvārs whom they themselves worshipped.

The first in this line was Nāthamuni or Śrī Raṅganātha Muni. A versatile scholar in Vedas and Smṛtis, he was a Paramaikāntin who enjoyed the arcā-avatāra of the Lord. He divided the Prabandhams into four parts of 1000 verses each, classified them according to different metres and introduced them to be sung in various Śrīvaiṣṇava shrines including the Śrīraṅgam temple. The installation of the images of Āḻvārs in temples and the conduct of the recitation of the Vedas and the Prabandhams alike, soon spread throughout the Śrīvaiṣṇava world.

The elevation of Tamil works to the status of the Vedas in the temple festivals in turn gave rise to a new and extensive type of religious literature in South India, an excellent admixture of Sanskrit and Tamil, the unique Maṇipravāḷa. In this intermingling of two different languages there is unity in diversity, each language retaining its individual identity and yet united in a new and appealing medium. Its main object was to expound the Tamil Vedas [] and reconcile their teachings with those of the Prasthānatraya. Thus the Tamil Prabandham became an essential part of Śrīvaiṣṇava literature. This in turn gave rise to numerous commentaries on Prabandhams requiring the authority of a great scholar to interpret them in the light of Śrutis and Smṛtis. This necessity, along with the need to expound and defend the Viśiṣṭādvaita system against rival schools of Śaṅkara, Madhva, and Buddha led to the establishment of the post of Ācārya “whose authority was law in religious worship and whose advice was the final word in temples and household alike.” In the early Śrīvaiṣṇava period, this pontifical head was usually entrusted with the management of the Śrīraṅgam temple. The early Ācāryas were successful in their great task because of the power they wielded over the important Śrīvaiṣṇava shrines of South India. But when this combination of offices ceased, Śrīvaiṣṇavism began to be divided into sects.58

It was but natural that when the dignity of the pontiff was established, the choice should fall on Nāthamuni and when he was formally appointed as the religious and administarative head in Śrīraṅgam temple, the whole Vaiṣṇava community paid homage to him. With him commenced a new era of activity and expansion in Śrīvaiṣṇavism. A great scholar and thinker, he composed two well-known works, the Yogarahasya and Nyāyatattva. None of

नाथोपज्ञं प्रवृत्तं बहुभिरुपचितं यामुनेयप्रबन्धैः त्रातं सम्यग्यतीन्द्रैरिदमिदमखिलं दर्शनं नः ॥59

Puṇḍarīkākṣa and Śrīrāmaṁiśra, the succeeding ācāryas, are not known to have composed any works, although some Taniyans (Tamil verses) are attributed to them. Then, in the Ācārya line, comes the illustrious grandson of Nāthamuni, Yāmunācārya, also known as Āḷavandār, meaning, ‘one who has come to rule the spiritual world’. His compositions include (a) the Catuślokī, which in four verses epitomises the position of Śrī as the consort of Lord Viṣṇu and the role played by Her in the salvation of the soul,

It was given to Yāmuna only to lay down the fundamentals of Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy and not to compose explanatory treatise (bhāṣya) on the Vedāntasūtras as Śaṅkara had done for Advaita. That task was achieved by Rāmānuja, said to have been named so by his maternal uncle who expected him to equal the great Lakṣmaṇa in his devotion to the Lord. Rāmānuja’s father was a disciple of Śrīśailapūrṇa, his brother-in-law, Yāmuna’s grandson and disciple who is said to have obtained from the Lord Himself the title of Tātācārya.60 The traditional date of Rāmānuja’s birth is 1017 A.D. and he is

Rāmānuja inherited three great tasks from his guru, Yāmuna - (i) the perpetuation of the memory of Parāśara (the author of the Viṣṇupurāṇa), (ii) the immortalisation of the glory of Nammāḻvār and (iii) the interpretation of Vyāsa’s Brahmasūtras according to the Viśiṣṭādvaita system. During his long life-span Rāmānuja more than carried out these injunctions. He learnt Vedānta from Tirukkoṭṭiyūr Nambi, Tiruvāymoḻi from Tiruvaraṅga Perumāḷ Araiyar (Āḷavandār’s son) and Tirumālai Āṇḍān; and Rāmāyaṇa from Periya Tirumalai Nambi. He is further said to have gone as far as Kashmir to see the Vṛtti or commentary of Bodhāyana which, according to tradition, interpreted the Brahmasūtras in the Viśiṣṭādvaita mode. These preparations being over, Rāmānuja gave the world in succession, several works which made him the foremost among the Śrīvaiṣṇava philosophers. One of his early works was the Vedārthasaṅgraha which deals in an elaborate manner with the teachings of the Upaniṣads. Rāmānuja attacks the Advaita interpretation of the mahāvākya “Tattvamasi”, Śaṅkara’s Māyāvāda and the Bhedābheda doctrines of Bhāskara and Yādavaprakāśa. He then reconciles the Vedic passages with one another and formulates Viśiṣṭādvaita. In the concluding section he proves beyond doubt that the supreme Deity is to be called Nārāyaṇa and not by any other name known to the Upaniṣads. His Vedāntadīpa and Vedāntasāra are [] comparatively shorter commentaries on the Brahmasūtras. His Gadyatraya comprising Śaraṇāgati-, Śrīraṅga- and Vaikuṇṭha- gadyas explains the concept of whole-hearted surrender called śaraṇāgati in this system, the glory of Lord Raṅganātha of Śrīraṅgam and the highest abode of the Lord (Śrīvaikuṇṭha). In his Nityagrantha, Rāmānuja explains the daily routine to be observed by a Śrīvaiṣṇava. His Gītābhāṣya, as the name suggests, is a commentary on the Bhagavad-gītā. He has bedecked the literary-cum-philosophical world with his Navaratna of which his magnum opus the Śrībhāṣya is the brightest gem. The Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy expounded by Rāmānuja in his Śrībhāṣya appealed to minds which were not satisfied with the Advaitic teachings of Śaṅkara. While Śaṅkara appealed to the head through his idealism and doctrine of Māyāvāda, Rāmānuja appealed to the heart through his realism and doctrine of loving devotion (bhakti) and self-surrender (prapatti). After completing his Śrībhāṣya Rāmānuja, toured extensively throughout the country to popularise his teachings.

Rāmānuja is said to have fulfilled his second vow of perpetuating Parāśara’s name, by calling upon Bhaṭṭa, a son of his friend and follower Kūreśa, who was also called Parāśara, to compose a commentary on the Viṣṇusahasranāma. This commentary, the Bhagavadguṇadarpaṇa (literally, the mirror reflecting the Lord’s auspicious qualities) is indeed the Viśiṣṭādvaita commentary on the Viṣṇusahasranāma, extolling the Lord’s [] agaṇitakalyāṇaguṇas as opposed to the Advaitic commentary of Śaṅkara stressing on the paratva of the Nirguṇa Brahman.

By authorising Kurukeśa, son of his uncle Śrīśailapūrṇa, to compose an authoritative commentary on the Tiruvāymoḻi as taught and expounded by him, Rāmānuja fulfilled his third vow of perpetuating Nammāḻvār’s name. This commentary is known as the “Six thousand “, and is the earliest one on the hymns of Nammāḻvār. It was the model upon which many compositions were written later, particularly in the maṇipravāḷa style.

Rāmānuja was not only a profound thinker, he was also a great organiser. His reform of the Śrīraṅgam temple administration is a significant landmark in the development of systematic worship in the Śrīvaiṣṇavite temples. He divided the Śrīvaiṣṇava world into a number of ācārya-dioceses, over each of which he appointed a pious householder as the head or siṁhāsanādhipati. A set of seventy-four spiritual leaders (ācāryapuruṣas) took up the work of teaching Viśiṣṭādvaita in the towns and villages of South India.

This move by Rāmānuja was an intermediate stage of informal organisations between the earlier system of personal choice of teacher-disciple that prevailed during the Nāthamuni - Rāmānuja period (9th or 10th century-early 12th century) and the period of the establishment of formal Maṭhas (14th [] century).61 It is not possible to trace in detail the entire succession in regard to these 74 ācāryas. Some families, however, still bear the names of these ācāryas like Embār, Nallān Cakravarti, Āḷkoṇḍa Villi and so on.

It was but natural that when these ācāryas started teaching Viśiṣṭādvaita in tune with Rāmānuja’s viewpoint, slightly varied interpretations according to individual perferences for Sanskrit and Tamil languages began to crop up. In course of time, these differences became marked (i.e. not merely abhiprāya and ācārabheda, but differences in ideas of prapatti, divine grace and position of Śrī) and gradually there emerged two major divisions in the Śrīvaiṣṇava community-the Northern school or Vaḍakalai and the Southern school or Teṅgalai. Here North and South are not in any way indicative of North and South India. Vaḍakalai indicates the school of thought prevalent in the northern part of the Tamil country with its cultural capital at Kāñcīpuram and Teṅgalai indicates the school of thought prevalent in the southern part of the Tamil country with its cultural capital at Śrīraṅgam. The usual and popular view is that the Vaḍakalai sect became distinct with Vedāntadeśika as its ācārya and the Teṅgalai sect became distinct with Maṇavāḷa Māmuṉi as its principal ācārya.

आनुकूल्यस्य सङ्कल्पः प्रातिकूल्यस्य वर्जनम्। रक्षिष्यतीति विश्वासो गोप्तृत्ववरणं तथा। आत्मनिक्षेप-कार्पण्ये षड्विधा शरणागतिः॥62

In this aṅga-aṅgi-samuccaya, ātma-nikṣepa alone is the aṅgin and the rest are aṅgas. This is proved beyond doubt by the following verse:

निक्षेपापरपर्यायो न्यासः पञ्चाङ्ग-संयुतः। सन्न्यासः त्याग इत्युक्तः शरणागतिरित्यपि॥63

For the Teṅgalais this prapatti is not qualified by any conditions. It is the only way for salvation. It is the first as well as the last way, and it can be

In conclusion it may be said that these differences were a natural result of the evolution in religious thought in the course of centuries. Neither the Āḻvārs nor the early Ācāryas spelt them out. In course of time this gave rise to subdivisions within Vaḍakalais, like the followers of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha, Śrī Āṇḍavan Āśrama, Parakāla Maṭha and within Teṅgalais like Vānamāmalai Maṭha and Yatirāja Maṭha. These divisions are not exhaustive, because numerous groups based on differences of deśācāra (local variations) tend to develop. The differences generally arise out of local traditions, affiliations to particular Ācāryas and Maṭhas and family practices. However it should be [] noted that the Pāñcarātra and Vaikhānasa Āgamas are not associated with Vaḍakalai and Teṅgalai sects, but cut across this division.

The following is the Vaḍakalai succession table originating from Lord Nārāyaṇa upto Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar, the founder of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha (1378 A.D.). It is not a family tree but rather guru-śiṣya lineage.64

Śrī Ahobilam

The Lord and the Kṣetra

उग्रोत्त्युग्र स्वरूप स्वभक्तजनां नुकेसरी। केसरीव स्वपोतानां अन्वेष्टा उग्रविग्रहः॥

The unique Man-Lion, Lord Narasiṁha has incarnated Himself in arcā form in several places. The most important among them is Śrī Ahobilam because according to our ancient scriptures (the Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa) this is the place where Narasiṁha avatāra and Hiraṇya-saṁhāra took place. The legend of the avatāra of Lord Narasiṁha is also told in the Bhāgavata-, the Vāyu-, the Padma- and the Kūrma- purāṇas, and in the Harivaṁśa. The Taittirīya Āraṇyaka contains the earliest allusion to Narasiṁha. The Nṛsiṁhapurāṇa begins with a salutation to Lord Narasiṁha and narrates the legend of Narasiṁha through sage Vyāsa’s disciple Romaharṣaṇa.

The presiding deity of Śrī Ahobilam is variously called – Narasiṁha, Nṛsiṁha, Ahobileśvara, Ahobila Narasiṁha, Ahobila Nātha. He is said to have emerged out of a pillar in Hiraṇyakaśipu’s palace on the fourteenth day of the bright fortnight of the Vaiśākha month (vaiśākhaśukla-caturdaśī). In Indian tradition that day is regarded as Narasiṁha Jayantī.65 The Lord is described in the Nṛsiṁhasahasranāmastotra as para-dharma, para-jñāna, and

The Lord in this avatāra is said to have lived in Śrī Ahobilam after killing Hiraṇyakaśipu. Traditional accounts relate that He seemed to have completely forgotten about Mahālakṣmī and His original abode, Śrī Vaikuṇṭha. Since Goddess Śrī cannot remain without Her Lord, She devised a plan to join Him. The forest tribes who lived in and around Śrī Ahobilam are called Ceñcus. Goddesss Śrī took birth as the daughter of a Ceñcu chief. Enamoured by Her ravishing beauty, the Lord married Her and thus Lord Nārāyaṇa and the Universal Mother were united once more. Hence it is, that the consort of the Lord here is called Ceñcu Lakṣmī. As a result, the Ceñcu tribes became ardent votaries of Lord Viṣṇu.

Location

Ahobilam is one of the three sacred places in the long range of disjointed hills that go to make up that stretch of Eastern Ghats from Krishna river down south to the Tirupati Hills - called “Nalla Malais”. This range, nearly 250 miles long, starts from the sacred Śaivite temple Mallikārjuna - called “Śrīśailaparvatam” situated in the northern end of the river Krishna. The middle shrine is indeed Śrī Ahobilam, a Śrīvaiṣṇavite shrine of great [] antiquity. Tirupati or Tirumala is, of course, the Śrīvaiṣṇavite shrine of Lord Śrī Veṅkaṭeśvara at the southern end.

Ahobalam (Lo! what strength!) or Ahobilam (Lo! what a cave!) is a village and a shrine in the Nalla Malais (Black Mountain or in Tamil “Good Mountain”) in the Siruvel Taluk of Kurnool District between 15° 8’ N and 78° 45’ E. It has two parts namely Diguva (Lower) Ahobilam Temple at the foot of the hills, Eguva (Upper) or Pedda Ahobilam about four miles higher up, and a small shrine on the summit. While the Eguva temple is considered to be more important from the religious view-point, the Lower temple is architecturally more interesting. It contains beautiful panels from the Rāmāyaṇa inscribed on its walls. It has two great stone maṇṭapams supported by pillars, eight feet in circumference, hewn out of rock. Fergusson mentions that a Kalyāṇa Maṇṭapam (wedding hall) supported by 64 pillars (each of which is carved into several miniature pillars) is a fine specimen of architecture and though wanting in delicacy and elegance, is full of character and merit.66

This place has been sung by the Śrīvaiṣṇava saint, Tirumaṅgai Āḻvār in his mystic outpourings. It is one of the 108 Divya Deśas praised by the

Ādi Śaṅkara praises this deity in his two poems, Śrī Lakṣmīnṛsiṁha-karāvalamba-stotra and Śrī Lakṣmīnṛsiṁhapañcaratnastotram. The Guru-paramparāprabhāvam speaks of Nammāḻvār’s visit to this place in search of Lord Narasiṁha:

ஆடியாடி யகம் கரைந்து, இசை பாடிப்பரவிக் கண்ணீர் மல்கி, எங்கும் நாடிநாடி நரசிங்கா வென்று வாடிவாடு மிவ்வா னுதலே.67

Rāmānuja’s visit is also found mentioned in the Guruparamparāprabhāvam Āṟāyirappaṭi, as also that of Vedāntadeśika and Nayinārācārya. Rāmānuja is said to have issued an edict that Narasiṁha worship should be done daily by all Siṁhāsanādhipatis. Vādikesari Aḻagiya

Tradition has it that it is the idol form of the Lord that was worshipped by the Vibhava form of the same Lord, in Śrī Rāma Avatāra and Lord Śrī Veṅkaṭeśvara (Lord of Seven Hills). It is said that Lord Śrī Rāma worshipped Lord Narasiṁha in this place and sang five verses in His praise. It is known as Narasiṁha Pañcāmṛtam found in the Śeṣa Dharma, 47th chapter of the Harivaṁśa. It is said that only after such a worship did Lord Śrī Rāma get information about Śrī Sītā’s whereabouts which ultimately led to Rāvaṇavadha and reunion with Sītā Devī. In the Veṅkaṭācalamāhātmya it is mentioned that Lord Śrīnivāsa and Goddess Padmāvatī worshipped the Lord at Ahobilam and were then united in divine wedlock. Hence, like Śrīraṅganātha, Lord Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha is also called “Periya Perumāḷ” - the great God or God of gods.

The Holy shrine of Ahobilam is termed as Navanṛsiṁha-kṣetram as nine forms of Lord Narasiṁha are housed in these forest shrines.

The hills of Ahobilam or Siṅgavelkuṉṟam as the Āḻvārs extolled this place, symbolise the trunk of Ādi-śeṣa, Lord Viṣṇu’s serpent, its hood being Tirumala and its tail Śrīśaila.

Of the nine Narasiṁha shrines, the Mālola Narasiṁha is the Ārādhyadevatā of the Jīyars of Ahobilam. This idol is said to have been installed by Sage Bharadvāja. It is significant in that it is the śāntarūpa of the Lion-God. We have the Lokamātā Mahālakṣmī seated on the lap of Her pacified Lord - the Lord who delights or pleases the Universal Mother. The Lord is Lakṣmyā samāliṅgitavāmabhāga, for bhaktānugraha.

The Greatness of Narasiṁha Avatāra

It is a well-known fact that the vibhava form (avatāra) of Śrīman Nārāyaṇa descending from Śrīvaikuṇṭha to this mundane world is for duṣṭanigraha and śiṣṭaparipālana. In the Lord’s mind, the śiṣṭaparipālana is given pride of place by the Lord’s own words in the Bhagavad Gītā (IV. 8):

परित्राणाय साधूनां विनाशाय च दुष्कृताम्।

नरवत् सिंहवत् चैव रूपं यस्य महात्मनः।68

the beatific and beautiful Man-Lion form. It incorporates the “unity in diversity” principle very aptly. Here the two apparently diverse elements - the ugra and the bhadra nṛsiṁhas are interfused into this unique form.

Let us consider the circumstances leading to this glorious avatāra. We have the akiñcana-bhakta-dānavaśiśu Prahlāda unarmed, standing before the terrifying demon Hiraṇyakaśipu, and chanting with immense and intense faith, harināma and harināma alone, though taunted and tormented by his own father to recite his name. Undaunted the child stood, spurning all material wealth and pleasures, ready to face innumerable travails for the sake of his Lord Hari and declaring in unequivocal terms that Lord Hari alone is omnipotent, omnipresent and omniscient, and that no power in the world can disprove it. It is no wonder that the Lord (becomes a slave as it were, to such a devotee who has mahāviśvāsa) rushed to his succour unasked for, and impregnated all things great and small, tṛṇa and stambha alike with the

स्तम्भस्य दानवशिशोः परिपालनाय भद्रां नृसिंहघटनामधिजागृषस्ते। स्तम्भैकवर्जमधुनपि करीश! नूनं त्रैलोक्यमेतदखिलं नरसिंहगर्भम्॥69

One other aspect of this avatāra which Vedāntadeśika highlights is that the Lord removed the odium of childlessness even in the case of an inanimate object the stambha; and in this process, enabled it to become a grandmother of Brahmā.

या काचित् सहसा महासुरगृहस्थूणा पितामह्यभूत्।70

This avatāra exhibits a unique combination of the Lord’s paratva and saulabhya - the para aspect of the Lord (delineating the ugrasiṁha aspect),

सटापटलभीषणे सरभसाट्टहासोद्भटे स्फुरत्क्रुधि परिस्फुटद्भृकुटिकेडऽपि वक्त्रे कृते।71

कृपा कपटकेसरिन् दनुजडिम्भदत्तस्तना सरोजसदृशा दृशा व्यतिविषणज्य ते व्यज्यते॥72

showing the motherly affection of the same Lord towards the youngest and perhaps His greatest devotee Prahlāda, suckling him with the “milk of human kindness”. The Divine grace and fury functioning in the same avatāra simultaneously and in the same organ (the Eyes) has been pointed out beautifully by none other than kavisiṁha Vedāntadeśika who highlights the Lord’s aghaṭita-ghaṭanā-sāmarthya (capacity to bring together the incompatible and the impossible). Vedāntadeśika uses almost similar (same) words in his Dayāśatakam (84) also:

दये वृषगिरीशितुर्दनुजडिम्भदत्तस्तना सरोजसदृशा दृशा समुदिताकृतिर्द्दश्यसे॥

Śrīvatsāṅkamiśra (Kūratāḻvāṉ) has extolled this unique Man-Lion avatāra. Born out of a pillar, neither man nor beast and yet both man and lion—His very avatāra bespeaks His supremacy and transcendance. These extraordinary features certainly do give away His paratva.

नरसिंहतनुर्गुणी समसमयसमुद्भवश्च भक्तगिरः। स्तम्भे च सम्भवस्ते पिशुनयति परेषतां वरद!॥73

स्वभक्त्यन्तरायनिवारणाय प्रदर्शिता। तत् क्षणप्रणतिपत्र यथाकामदिदृक्षया महानृसिंहसंहननः

and adds that He is Śrīmān74 -

परस्पर-दुर्धर-अपूर्व-विकृतस्वरूप-परिग्रहादप्रिश्रीमान् - शौन्दर्यलावण्यादिभिः अतिमनोहरादिरूपः।

Tirumaḻiśai Āḻvār (Bhaktisāra) expresses this idea in a similar strain75 - “because He assumes the form of the Lion, He is beautiful.” One cannot look for a better credential for the charm and beauty of Lord Narasiṁha than what has been said by Godā (Āṇḍāḷ), when she dreams about her wedding and relates that dream to her friend and refers to the form of Narasiṁha twice viz. (i) when she enters the marriage hall76 and (ii) when He holds Her hands above His, during the lāja-homa.77 He was “Kāle Nṛsiṁha”78 to Rukmiṇī Devī, in her love-epistle to Lord Kṛṣṇa. This avatāra stands as a class apart in that it was

Another fact that surfaces from this glorious avatāra is that the Lord’s compassion, all-encompassing as it is, extends even to the demon king Hiraṇyakaśipu who hated Him most. Obsessed by thoughts of Lord Hari, His Greatness and Immanence, he uttered the Lord’s Name with hatred and fear. Ironically this earned liberation for him in the end.

It will be fitting to conclude this chapter with the following second śloka of the Ārtatrāṇa-parāyaṇa Nārāyaṇastotra:79

प्रह्लादार्ति यदीश्वरो व्रद हरिः सर्वत्र मे दर्शय स्तम्भे चैनमिति ब्रुवन्तमसुरं तत्राविरासीद्धरिः। वक्षस्तस्य विदारयन् निजजने वात्सल्यमावेदयन् आर्तत्राणपरायणः स भगवान् नारायणो मे गतिः॥

SAṀNYĀSA DHARMA IN ŚRĪVAIṢṆAVISM

The contribution of holy persons and saṁnyāsins (termed variously as sages, saints, ascetics) to the development of world religions and thought is invaluable. The Indian panorama, fused as it is with variegated cultures and religions provides an unending line of these enlightened souls (Śaṅkara, Rāmānuja, Madhva, Mahāvīra, Buddha and so on) who spurned material pleasures and worked for the service of humanity. It is to these ascetics that India owes largely all that is valuable and enduring in her cultural and spiritual life.

The saṁnyāsa tradition or asceticism is not new to India. S. Radhakrishnan observes that from the beginning of her history, India has adored and idolised not soldiers and statesmen, not men of science and industry, not even poets and philosophers, but those rare and chastened spirits – the saints or ascetics. The spectacle of these holy souls walking from one corner of the country to the other through dust and din, in rain and sunshine, unmindful of the privations and ever seeking the goal of the divine and spreading the message of the divine is a familiar sight from days of yore. The concept of asceticism has so much fascinated the western mind that the French philosopher Renou in his Religions of Ancient India considers Hinduism as the very type of a religion of renunciation.80 J.C. Oman in The Mystics, Ascetics

According to the Smṛtis, a dvija (twice-born) should pass through the four stages of life:of a student (Brahmacārin), a householder (Gṛhastha), a hermit (Vānaprastha) and finally an ascetic (Saṁnyāsin). In days of yore, the fourth stage was permitted only to old people after a well-spent life.81 Later, a Brahmacārin was admitted into the order, if his tongue, emotions, appetite and speech are pure and under strict control.82 The Jābālopaniṣad allows the option to regard saṁnyāsa as the fourth stage after any of the first two āśramas. Owing to the great similarity between the third and fourth stage, the third stage came to be gradually ignored and people passed directly from the life of a householder to that of an ascetic.

In the Śrīvaiṣṇava tradition, this āśrama is considered as a very exalted stage of one’s life and is assigned to the final stages of one’s existence. This system has been sanctified by Yāmunācārya, Rāmānuja, Embār, Nañjīyar, Periya Jīyar, Brahmatantrasvatantra Jīyar, Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar, Rāmānuja

The ascetic is a symbol of holy life. One who enters the superior order of an ascetic not only separates himself from all temporal ties and carnal affections, but also rises superior to the householder (gṛhastha) who conforms to the rites and ceremonies enjoined by ordinary karmakāṇḍa. Sage Yājñavalkya states that the difference between a saṁnyāsin and a gṛhastha is comparable to the difference between the resplendent sun and the firefly; the difference between the huge Mt. Meru and the minute mustard seed.

सूर्यर्षभयोर्यद्वदन्तरं मेरुसर्षपयोर्यथा। अन्तरं हि महद् दृष्टं तथा भिक्षुगृहस्थयोः॥83

He is a saṁnyāsin because he is a sarvasaṅga parityāgī i.e. he abandons all earthly possessions – father, mother, wife, son and daughter. It is a total secesssion from domestic life followed by certain purificatory

कामः क्रोधस्तथा दर्पो लोभमोहादयश्च ये। तांस्तु दोषान् परित्यज्य परिपूर्णमयो भवेत्॥

रागद्वेषवियुक्तात्मा समलोष्टाश्मकाञ्चनः। प्राणहिंसानिवृत्तश्च मुनिः स्यात्सर्वान्निरस्पृहः॥

दम्भाहङ्कारनिर्मुक्तो हिंसापैशून्यवर्जितः। आत्मज्ञानगुणोपेतो यतिर्मोक्षमवाप्नुयात्॥

Sage Jamadagni declares that even the external guise of a saṁnyāsin has its own greatness84 –

दुर्बलो वा सुवृत्तो वा मूर्खः पण्डित एव वा। काषायमात्रवेषेण (दण्डमात्रेण) यतिः पूज्यो युधिष्ठिर॥

यत्र भिक्षुगृहं तत्र तं च मां विद्धि पाण्डव।

“Wherever you see a saṁnyāsin, Oh Pāṇḍava! know him to be Myself.”

Through this we come to know that the saṁnyāsin is of the nature of Lord Viṣṇu (viṣṇusvarūpa). The same point is brought out by Sage Dattātreya:85

त्रिदण्डिस्वरूपविप्रः साक्षान्नारायणात्मकः। यस्तु पूजयते भक्त्या विष्णुस्तेन प्रपूजितः॥ द्वे रूपे वासुदेवस्य चलं चाचलमेव च। चलं संन्यासिनां रूपम् अचलं प्रतिमादिकम्॥

“A tridaṇḍi saṁnyāsin is none other than Lord Nārāyaṇa and one who worships a saṁnyāsin is actually worshipping Lord Viṣṇu Himself. Lord Vāsudeva has two forms - one moving and the other fixed. The moving form is saṁnyāsin and the fixed form is the idol form (arcā rūpa) as worshipped in temples.”

The Yatiliṅgasamarthana of Vātsya Varadācārya is in three skandhas and presents the characteristics, qualifications and duties of the four classes of Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetics. It is based on the Śāṭyānakopaniṣad and Smṛti texts like

Quoting Hārīta, Vātsya Varadācārya states:

चातुर्विध्यं भिक्षवस्तु स्मृताः सामान्यलिङ्गिनः। तेषां पृथक् पृथङ्नाम वृत्तिभेदात् कृतं श्रुतौ॥ कुटीचको बहुदकः हंसश्चैव तृतीयकः। चतुर्थः परमो हंसो यो यः पश्चात् स उत्तमः॥ लिङ्गं तु वैष्णवं तेषां त्रिदण्डं स पवित्रकम्॥86

The Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetics fall into four groups namely Kuṭīcaka, Bahūdaka, Haṁsa and Paramahaṁsa, each latter one being superior to the previous one.

Saṁnyāsa is to be resorted to in the Uttarāyaṇa, when the sun is in its northward course. The intendant has to find an ascetic possessed of the virtue of quiescence. He should observe him and note for three months the duties of the ascetic. He should purify himself with the chanting (japa) of the sacred Gāyatrī and should perform a sacrifice to Prajāpati. According to the Yajurveda,87 he should be a tridaṇḍin. Hārīta88 states:

त्रिदण्डं वैष्णवं सोम्यं सत्त्वञ्च समपर्वकम्। वेष्टितं कृष्णगोबालरज्ज्वा तु चतुरङ्गुलम्॥

A tridaṇḍin according to Manu89 is one who has restraint over his speech, mind and body, and does not imply mere physical appearance of carrying a tridaṇḍa.

वाग्दण्डोऽथ मनोदण्डः कायदण्डस्तथैव च। यस्यैते निहिता बुद्धौ त्रिदण्डीति स उच्यते॥

A saṁnyāsin should always possess the following five things: yajñopavīta, tridaṇḍa, jalapavitra, kaupīna and kaṭivastra.90

उपवीतं त्रिदण्डं च पात्रं जलपवित्रकम्। कौपीनं कटिवस्त्रं च न त्याज्यं यावदायुषम्॥

The tridaṇḍa itself is viṣṇurūpa and has to be carried always.

विष्णुरूपं त्रिदण्डाह्वयं सर्वथा धारयेद्यतिः।

A Pāñcarātra text, Tattvasāgarasaṁhitā91 lays down thus –

त्रिदण्डंस्थाय्याग्रभागं तु संस्मरेत् परमेष्ठिनम्। द्वितीयं पुरुषमात्मानं विश्वात्मानं तु मध्यमम्॥ त्रिदण्डं समुदायमेवं विष्णुरूपं स्मरेद्यतिः।

The Vyūhamūrtis viz. Vāsudeva, Saṅkarṣaṇa, Pradyumna and Aniruddha, and finally Lord Nārāyaṇa are integrated into the tridaṇḍa. These

The śikhā or tuft is a necessity of a vaiṣṇava ascetic. Sage Śaunaka declares:

शिखावर्जं ऋतुसन्धिषु वापयेत्। न रोमाणि वापयेत्।

Hence Vātsya Varadācārya states:

अतः उपवीतादिवत् शिखापि चतुर्णामपि यतीनाम् अवश्यं धार्या।92

The sacred thread (yajñopavīta) is also very essential to the vaiṣṇava ascetic. Sages Śaunaka, Atri, Hārīta, Vṛddhajābāli and Dattātreya equivocally stress the necessity of donning the sacred thread by the saṁnyāsin; and they

Sage Aṅgiras states:

यतेर्लिङ्गं प्रवक्ष्यामि येनाऽसौ लक्ष्यते यतिः। बहुसूत्रं त्रिदण्डं च वस्त्रं जन्तुनिवारणम्॥ शिक्यं पात्रं बृसीश्चैव कौपीनं कटिवेष्टनम्। यथैतद्विद्यते लिङ्गं स यतिर्नेतरो यतिः॥93

Sage Uśanas declares:

बहुसूत्रपरित्यागात् ब्रह्मचारी गृही वनी। परिव्राड् वाऽपि पतति तस्मात् तन्न परित्यजेत्॥94

A saṁnyāsin has to perform śauca (purificatory functions) like brushing teeth, taking bath etc., just as a householder, but three or four times as much. He is to beg for his food and should eat only once in a day and only a specific quantity. He should eat food not to satisfy himself but only for his sustenance. He should bow to God and other elder ascetics (vṛddha-yatis), according to the rules of his order, but he should not pay obeisance to a householder however exalted the latter may be. The ascetic is not to utter any benediction when a person bows to him, but should only utter the name of Lord Nārāyaṇa. He should not mingle freely and should not speak in a flowery language which may attract people:

In the Nāradaparivrājakopaniṣad,95 the daily routine of an ascetic (yaticaryā) and the fruit thereof (tatphalam) are laid down:

सन्धिष्वे विषयांणां यः समर्थोऽविकलेन्द्रियः। सुप्तवद्वर्तते नित्यं स भिक्षुर्गुम्ध उच्यते॥ रागं द्वेषं मदं मायां द्रोहं मोहं परात्मसु। षडेतानि यतिर्नित्यं मनसाऽपि न चिन्तयेत्॥

When a saṁnyāsin dies, he is to be buried in a pit and covered with salt. No mourning is to be observed and no ceremonies (śrāddhas) are to be performed except pārvaṇa on the eleventh day of his death.

The schism in Śrīvaiṣṇavism has left its marks on the ascetics too. A Vaḍakalai ascetic does not offer obeisance to a householder unless he happens to be the ascetic’s own Ācārya. He is not to touch any metallic vessels. He is expected to live only by accepting the specified quantity of food offered to him by orthodox Śrīvaiṣṇava Brahmins. The Teṅgalai ascetic, on the other hand, offers and receives obeisance even from a householder. He need not beg for his food and is allowed the use of metallic vessels of all kinds.96 He wears the upper cloth (uttarīya) up to the waist and another cloth on the waist.

All these are only minor differences and the Dharmaśāstras accord the ascetic a pre-eminent position and authority in religious matters. Whenever difficult questions arise about the validity of things from the religious point of view, the political heads generally consult the ascetics. Paul Deussen in his Philosophy of Upanishads97 observes, “the entire history of mankind does not produce much that approaches in grandeur to this thought enshrined in the saṁnyāsa dharma.”

The Maṭhas and Maṭhādhipati-yatis

The Maṭhas or religious institutions, ascetic in nature, were a continuation of an evolving pattern within Hinduism. Brahmanical Hinduism managed to have its way as long as there was no organised opposition to its tenets and way of life. In order to counteract the upsurge of heretical organisations and the onslaughts of Islam and Christianity, Hinduism had to adopt many methods. The Bhakti movement was one, the temple organisation was another, and the religious institutions or Maṭhas were the third. These three provided jointly the socio-religious framework needed to counter heretical religions with a proselytising mission.98 There is no basis in Hindu

The Vaiṣṇava Maṭhas were thus a continuation of an evolving pattern within Hinduism. Naturally there were differences in the set up and rules regarding the various Maṭhas – Śaiva Maṭhas of Dharmapuram, Tiruvāvaḍuturai, the Advaita Maṭhas of Śṛṅgerī, Pūrī, Kāñcī and the Śrīvaiṣṇava Maṭha of Ahobilam, Parakāla Maṭha, Śrīmad Āṇḍavan Āśrama, Vānamāmalai Maṭha and so on. One cardinal distinction is that the Śaiva Maṭhas are invariably presided over by the non-Brahmins, especially, “Caturthas” while the Advaita and Viśiṣṭādvaita Maṭhas are headed by Brahmins. The Advaita Maṭhas, on the analogy of Ādi Śaṅkara, are headed by celibates (naiṣṭhika brahmacārins) and never by householders or those who had been gṛhasthas.99

माठपत्यं यतिः कुर्यात् विष्णुधर्मभिवृद्धये। विष्णोर्निर्माल्यसेवाय वेदान्तव्यापनाय च। अन्यथा यः करोत्येवं स याति नरकं ध्रुवम्॥100

So, the rules are very strict. A maṭhādhipati yati should nurture Vaiṣṇava dharma, conduct daily worship of the Lord and Bhāgavatas and expound, establish and elaborate Vedāntas through discourses. He has to perform the four-month vow (cāturmāsyavrata). This vow should be begun on the Āṣāḍhapūrṇimā day. The rainy season begins from this month. During the rainy season, the roads and terrain will be infested with insects, and if the saṁnyāsin happens to travel during these months, he may incur the sin of killing these insects and worms. Hence it is ordained in the śāstras that the

प्रायेण प्रावृषि प्राणि সঙ্কुलं वर्त्म दृश्यते। अतस्तेषां अहिंसार्थं पश्चान् वै श्रुतिसञ्चयान्। स्थास्यामः चतुरो मासान् अत्रैवाऽऽस्तितं बाधके॥

This saṅkalpa is vital for the vow and hence it is that it is termed saṅkalpamahotsava. In the Vedas, it is said that a month can be considered to be a fortnight (pakṣa) and hence nowadays, the saṅkalpa is observed for four fortnights only.101

This vrata begins with Viṣvaksena ārādhana and then Bhūvarāha ārādhana is conducted, followed by prayers offered to Bhūdevī requesting Her for forgiveness for digging the mud. This is collected for bath and cleansing purposes for this period. This is termed mṛtsaṅgrahaṇam.

From the mud thus collected, two big balls are offered to the old ascetic reciting a mantra and then they should be taken back from him reciting another mantra, meaning, “May this mud protect me”.

Then, as formulated for the saṁnyāsins after kṣaura, mahānadīsnāna, mādhyāhnika duties and after worshipping iṣṭadevatā and ācārya, worship should be done with kumbha and chantings of pañcaśāntimantras; and then

निवसन्न्तु सुखेनऽऽत्र गमिष्यामः कृतार्थताम्। यथाशक्ति च शुश्रूषां करिष्यामो वयं मुदा॥102

After these preliminary rites, worship of the Lord should be performed by the ascetic. During the final celebrations after finishing kṣaura, mahānadīsnāna and anuṣṭhāna, the remaining mud should be mixed with water and disposed of.103

The Jīyars of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha belong to the paramahaṁsa category of ascetics and have been carrying on this saṁnyāsa tradition with great fervour. With this, our interest shifts to the Śrīvaiṣṇavite Maṭha of great fame and antiquity - the Ahobila Maṭha and the line of pontiffs or Jīyars who have adorned this sacred Maṭha from its inception in 1398 A.D. to the present day.

JĪYARS

THE JĪYARS OF ŚRĪ AHOBILA MAṬHA BIOGRAPHICAL DETAILS

This chapter is devoted to the study of the lives of the holy saints who have decorated the Pontificate of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha. Starting with Śrī Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa till the present Pontiff Śrīman Nārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika, 45 men of glory have occupied this position. The relevant biographical details are presented with a view to highlight the social, economic and political background in which they carried out their arduous task. The determination with which each and every Jīyar carried out the divine command of Śrī Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha by taking the Lord to the nooks and corners of the country in days of yore, when the transport and communication facilities were virtually non-existent, is to be appreciated.

1. Śrī Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

According to the ŚrīSannidhi-guru-paramparā also known as Satsampradāyamuktāvali,104 Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar, the first Pontiff of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha was the son of Śrī Kiḍāmbi Keśavācārya of Tirunārāyaṇapuram (Melkote) in Karnataka. He was born on Thursday, the fifth day of the bright fortnight (śuklapakṣapañcamī) of the solar Tamil month

Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar was named Śrīnivāsa with due reverence to the Lord of the Seven Hills. He received pañcasaṁskāra and early secular education at the feet of his father; and Ghaṭikāśatam Varadācārya (known also as Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ105 of Kāñcī took charge of his spiritual education. Śrīnivāsa was fully instructed in both Vedāntic and Prabandhic lore and soon was acclaimed as a great exponent of Rāmānuja-darśana.

The event of his life was his dream – the vision of Lord Lakṣmī Narasiṁha of Ahobilam who beckoned him to Ahobilam where he was to renounce the world even as a Brahmacārin, and was later to be the founder of the great Śrīvaiṣṇava Maṭha. Śrīnivāsa, a scholar of hardly twenty years of age could not grasp the true implications of the dream and hence consulted his ācārya Ghaṭikāśatam Varadācārya who asked him to obey the mandate. So, in the prime of his life, he wended his way to Ahobilam with a few followers. Bathing in the holy river Bhavanāśinī and after performing his nityānuṣṭhāna, the young Śrīnivāsa started meditating on Lord Nṛsiṁha. Then the Lord

Dream seems to have been the medium through which Lord Nṛsiṁha communicated with the Jīyar. He was asked to proceed to Āḻvār Tirunagari, the birth place of Nammāḻvār, on the banks of the Tāmraparṇi and pay obeisance to the Lord there. So, the Jīyar started his divyadeśayātrā, reached Āḻvār Tirunagari where, to his dismay, he found that it was named Ekādaśarudra-kṣetra (housing eleven liṅgas). The idol of Nammāḻvār was retrieved from a nearby tank106 by reciting the Kaṇṇinuṇ Śiṟuttāmpu (the prabandha by Madhurakavi Āḻvār). The local Śaivite Pāṇḍya king seems to have been converted to Śrīvaiṣṇavism and with his patronage, the idol of Nammāḻvār was consecrated and installed in the temple with due pomp and glory.107 Thereupon it is said that the presiding deity of Āḻvār Tirunagari

The Jīyar’s services were commemorated by the local chief by the installation of the Jīyar’s idol in a shrine built to the west of Nammāḻvār’s shrine within the temple. The Jīyars of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha are entitled to the “Mudal (first) Tīrtham”, “Śrī Śaṭhāri” and other respects due in the temple.

The incident in the Pāṇḍya king’s life finds a parallel in the life of the King of Orissa, Mukunda Devarāya by name, who was driven out of his kingdom by the Mohammedans. He resorted to the feet of the Jīyar and with the latter’s help was able to regain his lost kingdom. As a mark of gratitude and respect, King Mukunda Devarāya made a grant of chattra, cāmara, bherī, mṛdaṅga, elephants, horses and retinue to the Jīyar. He also ordered an artistic palanquin of ivory to be made for the Jīyar, in which he was taken out in procession. (This ivory palanquin is in vogue even today and after the paṭṭābhiṣekam of a new Jīyar, he is given the honour of being carried in this palanquin, in a procession.) Reference is made to this incident by the seventh

पूर्वं यस्तु मुकुन्ददेवविभुनारून्धे धृतान्दोलिकः प्राप्तः श्रीपुरुषोत्तमं रचितवान् भक्तप्रतिष्ठापनम्। लब्ध्वा दन्ति तुरङ्ग छत्रचामरन्द्रादीनि यमौ पुनश्च विजयी कर्णाटसिंहासनम्॥

The Jīyar then proceeded to Puruṣottamapurī (modern Pūrī) where he gave discourses on Rāmānuja-siddhānta and defeated many Advaita pandits in debates.108 Thus he was able to secure a large number of followers and ordered the installation of the images of Nammāḻvār and Rāmānuja in the temple at Pūrī.

The Jīyar then toured Kumbhakoṇam, Śrīraṅgam and Āḻvār Tirunagarī doing maṅgalāśāsana to the deities in the divyadeśas, administering pañcasaṁskāra and accepting śaraṇāgati of the devotees, and finally he reached his birth-place, Tirunārāyaṇapuram (Melkote). There, two important events are said to have taken place: (a) The presence of a five-hooded serpent as one of the audience during Śrībhāṣya-pravacana of the Jīyar, and (b) the

Then came the divine call from Kāñcī, the place of the Jīyar’s early education. Here, he ordered the construction of a massive hundred-pillared maṇṭapa and a kalyāṇamaṇṭapam for seating the Lord Varadarāja and Perundevi Tāyār during utsava days. Evidence of this contribution can be

The Jīyar then proceeded to Sholinghur expounding the philosophy of Rāmānuja. He met the grandfather of Doḍḍayācārya109 who learnt his lessons at the feet of this Jīyar. Pleased by the sincerity of his devotion, the Jīyar ordained him to take charge of the temple affairs at Sholinghur. It is a well

After worshipping Rāmānuja at Śrīperumbūdūr, the Jīyar proceeded to Śrīraṅgam. He was accorded a royal welcome with full temple honours, as a fitting climax, to half a century of strenuous toil of propagating the sampradāya. Here too, the Jīyar took upon himself the task of building the temple prākāra-walls (as was done in the past by Tirumaṅgai Āḻvār). In the northern entrance of Śrīraṅgam temple, he built a Maṭha and installed an image of Śrī Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha (and later the Jīyar’s image was also installed). He also built a frontal maṇṭapa for the Daśāvatārasannidhi at the northern suburb of Śrīraṅgam beyond the temple walls and installed the image of Tirumaṅgai Āḻvār. The idol of Vedāntadeśika was also duly consecrated in front of the Śrīraṅganācciyār sannidhi inside the temple precincts, and arrangements were made for the daily worship there. In fact, the Jīyar himself stayed for sometime there and carried on the activities of the Maṭha. In the Uttara street at Śrīraṅgam, a big Maṭha was constructed (near the eastern gate of the temple) and Lord Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha was installed. Having thus given fixity and method to the Maṭha activities and arranged for its continuance, the Jīyar felt himself called upon to proceed to Melkote and perform worship of Lord Tirunārāyaṇa. [] Here at his birth place, he consecrated the image of Vedāntadeśika, built a Maṭha and personally attended to the rituals of worship. The Jīyar was at this time, advanced in age and felt that his end was approaching.

With his usual great care and foresight, he selected Nampākkam Kṛṣṇamācārya Svāmī, the ‘Abhinava Vedāntadeśika’ as his successor. He bequeathed the saṁnyāsin’s robes and performed preṣamantropadeśa to him and named him Śrī Nārāyaṇa Jīyar and installed him as the second pontiff of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha. When we recapitulate the achievements of the Jīyar who had founded a great Śrīvaiṣṇava Maṭha and who, through his ceaseless peregrinations spread Rāmānuja-darśana far and wide, taking innumerable śiṣyas within his solacing fold, one cannot but marvel at the intensity of his devotion, determination of purpose, and his great organisational capability, next only to Rāmānuja himself.

The first Jīyar breathed his last at Melkote on Saturday, 11th day (Ekādaśī) of dark fortnight (kṛṣṇapakṣa) of Caitra month, Bahudhānya year, Kaliyuga 4560 equating to April 1458 A.D. His Bṛndāvan is at Melkote on the banks of Kalyāṇī Puṣkariṇī, an object of reverential worship for his numerous followers. His praises are being duly sung in beautiful verses (Aḻagiyasiṅgar Pugaḻ Mālai). [] His Taniyans (composed by the second Jīyar) are read as under:

प्रपद्ये निरवद्यानां निष्ठां गुणपदाम्पदाम्। शरणं भवभीतानां शठकोपाख्यनीश्वरम्॥ केशवार्यकृपापात्रं श्रीशमादिगुणार्णवम्। श्रीशठारियतीशानं देशिकेन्द्रमहं भजे॥

The First Jīyar in Contemporary Literature and Epigraphical Records

The accounts gleaned from the Guruparamparās do not furnish us with any reliable chronological or historical data. But we have satisfactory evidence in contemporary liter- ature and epigraphs which help us fix the times of the Jīyars.

Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar was the most revered scholar of his times. Many resorted to his feet for spiritual guidance. Maṇavāḷa Māmuṉi (1370-1443 A.D.) who is said to be an incarnation of Śrī Rāmānuja resorted to his feet and obtained preṣamantropadeśa and saṁnyāsāśrama from the first Jīyar. His other students were Yatirāja Jīyar, Śrīman Nārāyaṇa Jīyar, and Ācci Tiruveṅkaṭācārya.110

The famous Telugu poet who adorned the court of King Kṛṣṇadevarāya of Vijayanagar, Allasāni Peddanna (1509-1530 A.D.) is said to

विश्वास-टिप्पनी

नात्रोचितम् उक्तम्।
अहोबिलयतेर् एव कालः ६० वर्षेभ्योऽर्वाचीनः।

The T.T.D. Epigraphical Report says that the famous music composer, Tāḷlapākaṁ Annamācārya (1424-1503 A. D.) was a contemporary of the first

Jīyar. The songs of this famous composer inscribed on copper plates were found hidden in the underground cellars of the upper Ahobilam temple.111 One song reads as follows:112

चुट्टिदि रकु सुलभमुड हरि तोडु नीड यगु दोरमुनि यितडु केवल्यमुनकु गनक्य दापल म्रोवै द्रुतलकु दुदिपदमे पावन म्रुक रूपमे विरजकु नावै युन्नाडिदे यितडु कापाडग लोकमुल्लकु सुज्ञान दीपमे जगतिकि देजमे पापालडपन भवपयोधिुलकु तेपै युन्नाडिदे वीतडु कृपानिधिकि रंगापतिकि गाञ्चीवल्ल्कु वेकटगिरिपतिकि निरतिनहोबल नृकेसरिकि तत्परुडगु शठकोपमुनि यितडु

The Jīyar was a disciple of Nainārācārya the apostolic head from 1371 - 1410 A.D. Maṇavāḷa Māmuṉi (1370 - 1442 A.D.), Kaṇḍāḍai Rāmānuja Aiyaṅgār and the first, second and third Brahma Tantra Svatantra Jīyars of Parakāla Maṭha are said to have been the other contemporaries of the Jīyar.

Kaṇḍāḍai Rāmānuja Aiyaṅgār, a disciple of Aḻagiya Maṇavāḷa Jīyar and super- intendent of the free feeding house (Rāmānuja Kūṭam) at Tirumala

The first three Brahmatantra Svatantra Jīyars had been the heads of the Parakāla Maṭha. The date of the āsthāna of the first Jīyar is from 1364 - 1386 A.D.113 As per the Kāñcī Inscription dated in Śaka 1282 (29th March 1360 A.D.) he was the pontiff of the Maṭha.114 He was a direct disciple of Vedāntadeśika and Nainārācārya along with the first Jīyar of Ahobila Maṭha.

Sāḷuva Narasiṁha was a feudal king of Chandragiri who later extended his control over North Arcot, South Arcot and Chingleput districts, and finally usurped the throne of Vijayanagar.115 He is said to have installed the

An Inscription116 on a slab set up in a garden near Āḻvār Cheruvu (tank) in Tirupati, Chittoor District, refers to the building of Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha Maṇṭapa and laying out of a flower garden. It is dated in Śaka 1326 (1404 A.D.). It may be the Vaṇ Śaṭhakopan Nandavanam mentioned in a T.T.D. Inscription.

The second Inscription117 in Tamil on the western wall of the second prākāra in the Tirumala temple is the record of a śilāśāsana which contains among other things a provision that the balance of offerings be made to Aḻagiya Śiṅgar, his Ācārya, Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar. This is dated Śaka 1415 (15th June 1493 A.D.) when Sāḷuva Narasiṁha was ruling Vijayanagar. The above Inscription is particularly important for two things: The Jīyar was called Aḻagiya Śiṅgar and a disciple had endowed to perpetuate the memory of his Ācārya.

The third Inscription dated Śaka 1407 (6th June 1485 A.D.) records the execution of a śilāśāsana. It gives the information that Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar constructed the entrance of the Vasanta Maṇṭapa with stone through the

We also hear of Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Maṇṭapa in two other Inscriptions, one occuring at Tirumala118 dated Śaka 1427 (1.3.1505 A.D.) and one dated Śaka 1429 (4th October 1508 A.D.).119 K.V. Raman refers to an Inscription in Kāñcī Varadarāja temple dated Śaka 1431 (1509 A.D.) mentioning the gift of a land in a village Vaṇ Śaṭhakopapuram, named evidently after the first Jīyar.120

An Inscription in Kāñcī Varadarāja Temple dated 14.1.1517 A.D. mentions that King Kṛṣṇadevarāya visited Kāñcī in the course of his conquests, worshipped Lord Varadarāja and donated 1000 varāhas and granted certain villages for provisions of offerrings to the deity with the stipulation that the balance of offerings be utilised for distribution among the disciples of Śaṭhakopa Jīyar. This shows that the first Jīyar had established a

Two Inscriptions are found in Śrī Raṅganāthasvāmī temple, Śrīraṅgam. They mention King Kṛṣṇadevarāya’s visit to Śrīraṅgam and a provision for the distribution of a share of offerings to the disciples of Śaṭhakopa Jīyar Maṭha at Śrīraṅgam. All these are unmistakable attestations to the fame and popularity of the first Jīyar of Ahobila Maṭha.

The history of the Ācāryas of the Ahobila Maṭha who succeeded Śrī Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Svāmī is dealt with in the following pages. Lord Nṛsiṁha is referred to in Tamil as “Aḻagiya Śiṅgam” or “the Beautiful Lion”. As a natural corollary, the Ācāryas of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha who propogated the worship of Lord Nṛsiṁha are referred to as “Aḻagiya Śiṅgars”.

2. Śrī Nārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

Tirumalai Nampākkam Kṛṣṇamācārya, one of the disciples of the first Jīyar, succeeded him in the pontificate on the fifth lunar day of the bright half of Caitra in the year Bahudhānya in Kaliyuga 4560 (corresponding to Friday,

This Jīyar presided over the Maṭha for a period of fourteen years. He established an agrahāram at Madhuramaṅgalam. He installed an image of his Ācārya Śrī Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar in the temple at Melkote (Tirunārāyaṇapuram) and another in the Svāmī’s Bṛndāvanam at Melkote.

He attained Ācāryan Tiruvaḍi (liberation) at Tirunārāyaṇapuram in the year Nandana, in the Tamil month of Panguni, on Kṛṣṇapakṣa Dvitīyā tithi (March 1473). His Bṛndāvanam is located at Kalyāṇī Tīrtha at Melkote, beside that of his Ācārya.

His Taniyan reads as follows:-

श्रीशठारियतीशानपदपङ्कजषट्पदम्। श्रीमन्नारायणमुनिं श्रये श्रीभाष्यदेशिकम्॥

The Second Jīyar in Epigraphical Records

The second Jīyar appears in a few Inscriptions and these help us to fix his chronology. Two Tirupati Inscriptions121 falling within the reign of Kṛṣṇadevarāya give him a specific date. One of these occurring in the Govindarāja shrine in Lower Tirupati is a benefaction for the first Jīyar on his birthday (Puraṭṭāsi-Keṭṭai) the donor being Nārāyaṇa Jīyar himself. It is in both Tamil and Grantha. Therein, provision was made for five food services

The other Inscription122 dated Śaka 1438 (7th November 1516 A.D.) refers to the deposit of 3800 panams to be invested for the improvement of irrigation sources and the interest income thereof to be utilised for daily services in the Govindarāja temple. These Inscriptions show that the second Jīyar took certain steps to perpetuate the memory of his illustrious Ācārya by instituting services in his memory in the famous shrines at Tirupathi and Śrīraṅgam and popularising his Maṭha and carrying forward the work of propagation of Rāmānuja-darśana.

A third Inscription123 pertaining to this Jīyar is found in the South wall of the central shrine of Periyāḻvār temple at Alipiri. It records the execution of a Śilāśāsana in favour of the disciples of Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar for providing a certain offering in the Jīyar’s nandavana (flower garden).

Regarding the date 1516 A.D., S.K Aiyangar124 observes: “It is not given by way of a mere indirect reference but is the date of an actual record

If we are to accept these dates occuring in the Inscription as falling within the pontificate of Nārāyaṇa Jīyar, it is necessary to make an adjustment by putting up the dates of the Jīyars by about sixty years.125 The dates assigned to the Jīyars of Ahobila Maṭha are based on dates provided by the Śrī Sannidhi Guruparamparā. These dates, though disputed, are still given so that there may be a general chronological background for an account of the Jīyars.+++(5)+++

3. Śrī Parāṅkuśa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

This Jīyar was born in Malayāṅkuḷam agrahāram in the Kāñcīpuram District in the Tamil month of Thai under the asterism of Śravaṇa. His pūrvāśrama name was Kaṇḍāḍai Kṛṣṇayajvā, indicating that the Svāmī performed many yāgas. He assumed the office of pontificate on a full-moon day in Nandana year in the Tamil month of Paṅguni, corresponding to March 1473.

He established agrahārams at Nīrvalūr, Uragadam, Nimmili, Kāḍaṅgarai and Kaḷattūr. Then he proceeded to Tirukkōvalūr, Tiruvahin-drapuram, Śrīmuṣṇam and Śrīraṅgam. He stayed for sometime at Śrīraṅgam giving discourses on Grantha-catuṣṭayam. At the divine command of Rāmānuja he proceeded to Śrīmuṣṇam to worship the Lord Bhūvarāha. Having spent a worthwhile life, full of service to his pūrvācāryas and Lord Nṛsiṁha, the Svāmī attained Ācāryan Tiruvaḍi in the Tamil Month of Thai, Krodhi year (January 1485) on Kṛṣṇapakṣa caturdaśī. His Bṛndāvanam is at Śrīmuṣṇam. He occupied the pontificate for eleven years and ten months.

His Taniyan reads as follows:

श्रीमन्नाराणमुनेः पदपङ्कजषट्पदम्। पराङ्कुशमुनिं सम्यग् पराङ्कुशमुनिं भजे॥

4. Śrī Śrīnivāsa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

This Jīyar was born in the Tamil Month of Mārgaḻi under the star Citrā. He assumed office in the Tamil Month of Thai in the Krodhi year corresponding to January 1485 at Śrīmuṣṇam. [] This svāmī proceeded on his religous tours (sañcāra) from Śrīmuṣṇam to Śiṅgarkoil where he gave discourses on Ubhayavedānta texts. He then left for North India via Ahobila to Dvārakā, Gokulam, Mathurā, Avantī(Ujjain) and Ayodhyā and returned to Śiṅgarkoil via Kāñcī. Due to his failing health this Svāmī prayed to Lord Nṛsiṁha to relieve him of his mortal frame, after appointing a suitable successor. Complying to the Svāmī’s fervent prayer, Lord Nṛsiṁha appeared in the dream of a certain scholar (who was called “Sarvatantrasvatantra” in due recognition of his knowledge of all śāstras) and commanded him to proceed to Śiṅgarkoil to don the ochre robes. Accordingly, he proceeded to the Ahobila Maṭha at Śiṅgarkoil where the divine command was intimated to the Jīyar. It was thus that the fifth Jīyar126 was initiated into asceticism with the title of “Sarvatantra svatantra Śaṭhakopa Jīyar”. Subsequently, the elder Svāmī attained paramapada in the Tamil month of Citra in the year Pramādica on Kṛṣṇapakṣa caturthī (April

His Taniyan reads as follows:-

श्रीपराङ्कुशयोगीन्द्रचरणाम्बुजषट्पदम्। श्रीनिवासमुनिं वन्दे श्रीभाष्यमृतसागरम्॥

5. Kaṇḍalūr Śrī Sarvatantrasvatantra Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

This Jīyar was born in the Tamil month of Kārtigai under the star Kṛttikā in Ātreya Gotra. He assumed the pontificate in the year Pramādica in Citra month (April 1493) on Kṛṣṇapakṣa dvitīyā tithi.

An erudite scholar, well-borne out by his title “Sarvatantrasvatantra”, his discourses on religion and philosophy were most sought after by the Śrīvaiṣṇava community. He visited Śrīraṅgam and Tirumala, propagating Rāmānuja-darśana and proceeded to the North. He attained Ācāryan Tiruvaḍi in the year Kālayukti in the month of Thai (January 1499) on Kṛṣṇapakṣa navamī Tithi, on the banks of river Kṛṣṇā. His Bṛndāvanam is also situated there. He ruled over the pontificate for five years and nine months (1493 - 1499).127

श्रीनृसिंहदयापात्रं परवादिगजाह्वयम्। सर्वतन्त्रस्वतन्त्रायं शठकोपमुनिं भजे॥

Epigraphical Records

The T.T.D. Epigraphical Report mentions one Vedānta Śaṭhakopa Jīyar as the fifth pontiff who presided over the Ahobila Maṭha during the reign of Sadāśivarāya.

From the two Inscriptions in Lower Tirupati128 we understand that the Jīyar was the spiritual guru of the Nandyala royal family. This information is also contained in two other Inscriptions129 wherein it is stipulated that the donor’s share of the offering (prasāda), be made over to their Ācārya.

The T.T.D. Epigraphical Report also states, “this is the same Vedānta Śaṭhakopa Jīyar who regained for Gajapati king, Mukunda Devarāya, his lost kingdom.”

6. Śrī Ṣaṣṭha Parāṅkuśa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

This Jīyar was born in the Tamil month of Thai under star Pūraṭṭādi in Karalappākkam village near Tiruvaḷḷūr in Bharadvāja Gotra. In his pūrvāśrama days, he was well-known in literary circles as Vidvān Vaṅgīpuram Veṅkaṭācārya Svāmī.

This Jīyar was a famous personage of his times. Being well grounded in Jñāna and Anuṣṭhāna, he had a large number of followers and many resorted to His Holy Feet for pañcasaṁskāra and prapatti.

Ṣaṣṭha Parāṅkuśa was a contemporary of King Sadāśiva Rāya according to the Śrī Sannidhi Guruparamparā. It is stated that the Jīyar with his superhuman130 powers was able to cure the daughter of the King from the evil effects of certain spirits. As a mark of respect, the King visited Ahobilam and paid obeisance to the Lord and the Jīyar. He also gifted the village of Śrī Bhāṣyapuram on the banks of river Pinākinī. The royal patronage was one of the causes for the Ahobila Maṭha gaining in prominence and prosperity during this period.

श्रीमच्छठारिमुनिपादसरोरुहंसं श्रीमत्पराङ्कुशपयोधनलब्धबोधम्। श्रीमन्नृसिंहवरदं पदमावदान्तं श्रीमत्पराङ्कुशमुनिं प्रणतोऽस्मि नित्यम्॥

Epigraphical Records

Fortunately, Parāṅkuśa II is mentioned in many Inscriptions, which help us to fix his chronology.

Of the twenty-three Inscriptions131 at Lower Ahobilam, thirteen fall within the period of King Acyuta Rāya and King Sadāśiva Rāya (1513-1564 A.D.). Six Inscriptions132 at Upper Ahobilam belong to King Sadāśiva Rāya’s era (1548-1564 A.D.). Parāṅkuśa Jīyar is specifically referred to in three Inscriptions at Lower Ahobilam133 and two at Upper Ahobilam.134 Mention is made of him in two Inscriptions135 as the Trustee of the Ahobilam Temple. The Inscriptions at Lower Ahobilam bear the dates in Śaka 1472 (1550 A.D.) and Śaka 1477 (1555 A.D.). Those at Upper Ahobilam are dated in Śaka 1480 (1558 A.D.) and Śaka 1476 (1564 A.D.). Thus we find that the pontificate of Parāṅkuśa Jīyar II falls between 1550 A.D. and 1564 A.D. and entirely within the reign of King Sadāśiva Rāya. K.V. Raman refers to two

Śrī Vāhinipati, the poet-laureate, has praised this Jīyar in his Guruparamparā Pāramārśa wherein he refers to the Jīyar as the foremost among Saṁnyāsins, second only to Ādiśeṣa, third behind Bṛhaspati and Śukra, and fourth behind the Holy Trinity, viz. Śiva, Viṣṇu and Brahmā.136 Ātreya Ahobila Guru praises the Jīyar as a Sarvatantrasvatantra in his work, Periya Vāccāṉ Piḷḷai’s Rāmāyaṇa Pratyeka Śloka Vistaraṇam. He also eulogises the Jīyar for installing the images of Āḻvārs in many places.

7. Śrī Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

This Jīyar was born in Iḷaṅgāḍu (agrahāram) village in Kāñcī District in Bhāradvāja Gotra in the Tamil month of Vaikāśi under star Viśākhā. He assumed the office of pontificate in the Tamil month of Māsi in Āṅgirasa year ( February, 1513) in his twenty-fifth year. He visited many holy shrines from Ahobilam to Rāmeśvaram. Because of his erudition, he was honoured with the titles of “Śatalekhinī lekhānuguṇa kavitā dhuranthara, kavitārkika kaṇṭhīrava”. He wrote a five-act drama in Sanskrit titled “*Vāsantikā-

The Jīyar visited many divyadeśas including Tirumala, Kāñcī, Śrīraṅgam, Tirunārāyaṇapuram, Kumbhakoṇam, Āḻvār Tirunagari and stayed at Śrīraṅgam giving religious discourses. He attained Ācāryan Tiruvaḍi in the year Viṣu, in the Tamil month of Paṅguni on a pūrṇimā day at Śrīraṅgam (April, 1522). He ruled the pontificate for nine years and two months (1513-1522). His Bṛndāvanam is at Śrīraṅgam.

His Taniyan reads as follows:

श्रीपराङ्कुशयोगीन्द्रचरणाम्बुजसेखरम्। समस्तशास्त्रपारीणं शठकोपमुनिं भजे॥

8. Śrī Parāṅkuśa Yatīndra Mahādeśika

This Jīyar was born in the Tamil month of Mārgaḻi under star Aśvinī. He was initiated into the ascetic order by his pūrvācārya and he took charge as the spiritual head of the Ahobila Maṭha in the Viṣu year, in the month of Paṅguni (April 1522) on a Śukla ekādaśī day. He stayed at Śrīraṅgam for the rest of his life, giving discourses on religious and the philosophical texts like

His Taniyan reads as follows:

शठकोपयतिनेत्रद्वयपदपङ्कजषट्पदम्। सर्वशास्त्रार्थतत्त्वज्ञं पराङ्कुशमुनिं भजे॥

9. Śrīman Nārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika
