The aerial chariot then takes a different route and proceeds to Sahyādri, which is described to be the abode of rare and fragrant herbs and trees (v .222) capable of rejuvenating human life and hence the mountain is always flocked by practitioners of Siddha medicine (v. 223), delighted by the fragrance of sandal wood and the beautiful foliage all around.

[] The sight of Ayodhyā towards the North, sends Vijaya into raptures and he declares that this town has gained in sanctity because Lord Rāma ruled over it (v. 229) and He is verily Lord Nārāyaṇa, Śrīpatiḥ who has the whole world as His body (v. 234). Then Jaya intervenes to enquire whether Rāma was an incarnation of Śiva (v. 235). Vijaya speaks of the greatness of the Epic Rāmāyaṇa in (v. 236) and points out that Rāma should not be taken to mean Śiva, because the very terms Viṣṇu and Nārāyaṇa are used in relation to Rāma at the beginning, middle and end of the Epic; and by applying the rule of upakrama and upasaṁhāra in tātparyanirṇaya, these names are synonymous and mean only Lord Nārāyaṇa.

In the following five verses he speaks of the glory of Rāmāvatāra and refers to the story wherein Śiva was petrified through the huṅkāra of Lord Viṣṇu (v. 241). Reference is also made to Lord’s Śiva’s reply208 to Pārvatī who wanted to know the easy way to recite the thousand names of Lord Viṣṇu:

केनोपायेन विष्णोरपि खलु लधुना नामसहस्रपाठौ मर्त्यानां बोधवीति प्रिय वद तदिति प्रष्टुमुद्यामुयाम्। तत्पुत्थं रामनामग्रहणामिति हस्योक्तिन्नेषा धुनीते त्वच्छुद्धाशृङ्गं तस्मात् रघुपतिरनघो विष्णुपूर्णवतारः॥ (v. 244)

Vijaya glorifies the potency of reciting the Rāmanāma (v. 247) with beautiful alliteration. In a string of 8 verses (Rāmāṣṭakam) the author with his mastery of the Sankrit vocabulary extols the greatness of Kosalendra Rāma.

[] He is the supporter of the universe (dhātā lokasya) always the winner in battles (v. 248 jetā raṇabhuvi) and he is the source of innuverble auscipious qualities. The phalaśruti for reciting the Rāmāṣṭakam is also stated. It confers always auspiciousness and long life and removes the greatest of sins. Jaya is puzzled as to how Lord Rāmā is thus praised, though he was the cause of destruction of innumerable rākṣasas. Vijaya brushes aside this query and says that the wicked should be punished at all costs and Lord Rāmā is the destroyer of enemies (virāmāḥ śatrūṇām). Reference is made to the term śaraṇāgati as bharanyāsa (v. 255) when Sugrīva resorted to Lord Rāmā’s feet seeking for protection from Vāli’s attack.

From Ayodhyā they proceed northwards and the River Gaṅgā is described in all its splendour in 10 verses. The sanctifying powers of the river is highlighted and it is mentioned that Lord Śiva’s greatness is enhanced because of his association with this river

बर्हेन्द्रद्राघीयमान् भवति पशुपतिः सर्वविद्द्वत्समीड्यः। नूनं त्वत्सङ्गमामात् ... (v. 269.)

In v. 271, the author states that those who resort to the Lord for freedom from bhavabhāva (worldly existence) attain Bhavabhāva (become Śiva). This is an example of virodhābhāsa alaṅkāra.

भवाभावमम्ब परिहर्तुमादरात् भवमुपेत्य भवभावमिच्छति।

[] The holy city of Kāśī is described in 4 verses. When Jaya enquires that it is Lord Śiva’s abode (śiva kṣetram iyaṁ kāśī) Vijaya categorically states that it is because of the intense associations of Lord Viṣṇu with this place that it is the cause of liberation and it is in this vey place that Lord Śiva was conferred boons by Lord Rāma. In the phalaśruti it is stated that this Upaniṣadic secret meaning when heard by people, will purify them through the grace of Lord Rāmabhadra.

The holy city of Dvārakā is described next, in 4 verses. They pay their obeisance to Lord Kṛṣṇa (Dvārakāpurīśa):

कृष्णाकृतेः भगवतः चरणारविन्दं वृष्णा शिवेन विधिनापि समर्च्यमानम्। तृष्णापहं जनिपथम्रपिणां नराणां मुष्णातु मामकमुदं हृदयान्धकारम्॥ (v. 280)

With this Jaya and Vijaya return to their heavenly abode. The colophon of the text states that the author Vātsya Vīrarāghava has written this Vaikuṇṭhavijayacampū which is a precious gem among campūs to please Lord Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha.

It is stated in the preface to this book that this campū is modelled on the lines of Viśvaguṇādarśacampū by Veṅkaṭādhvarin and many points of similarity are brought out. Similarity with Vedāntadeśika’s Daśāvatāra stotra and Raghuvīragadya is also discernible. The author’s scholarship and literary and poetic skill in the use of yamaka and anuprāsa is seen throughout the

[] work. Later, ācāryas209 opine that this work is a fitting reply to Nīlakaṇṭha Dīkṣita’s Nīlakaṇṭha Vijaya Campū. The supremacy of Lord Viṣṇu, the philosophy of Viśiṣṭādvaita wherein the whole universe forms the body of the Lord, the efficacy of śaraṇāgati, the necessity of performing kaiṅkarya and loving devotion to the Lord and His devotees, the greatness of ācāryas and their duties as also those of pontifical heads are all presented through this medium of campū with its beautiful admixture of terse prose and simple verse form. To a student of literature, the graphic descriptions of many holy places of worship are a source of sustaining interest. Poetry and prose, religion and philosophy are all brought together in an enjoyable confluence in this work.

Adhikaraṇakalpataru or Padayojanā

210

The Padayojanā is an explanatory treatise on Śrī Vedāntadeśika Adikaraṇasārāvalī. The author has taken into consideration the commentary Cintāmaṇi of Śrī Kumāra Varadācārya on Adhikaraṇasārāvalī; but feels that this was not an exhaustive explanation. So, the Padayojanā has been written to elucidate the meanings so that even a layman who is interested to know about the adhyātma śāstra, will be able to grasp its import. The title

[] Padayojanā is quite fitting in the sense that each pada has been clearly explained with the padaccheda, padārthokti and padāśo’rthaprakāśana.

In the invocatory verse, the author pays homage to the Lord of Ahobilam and then to his ācārya and then to Vedāntadeśika and his son Kumāra Varadācārya. He calls himself Rāmāvāsarāghavācārya as his pūrvāśrama name was Śrīnivāsa Rāghavācārya Svāmī.

In the preface the 41st Jīyar (under whose auspices this book was published) opines that the two commentaries Cintāmaṇi and Padayojanā should be read along with Vedānta deśika’s Adhikaraṇasārāvalī as they form the conch (śaṅkha) and discus (cakra) of Deśika’s poetic piece that is Brahman.211 The preface is significant in that it includes a brief sketch of Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa’s life as also that of the author of Padayojanā (Attipaṭṭu Jīyar as he was popularly called), the basic texts of the Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy and a list of the works of the Jīyar.

Explaining the first word svasti of the benedictory verse of the Adhikaraṇasārāvalī, the author states that it is āśīḥ kṣemaṅkara śāsanaviśeṣam and quotes the lexicon - āśīḥ kṣemapuṇyādau. For the term

[] śāśvat (which was not explained by Kumāra Varadācārya) our author explains - punaḥ punaḥ, triṁśadvāram śrāvitāśarīrakabhāṣyaḥ ityādyukta-rityā bahuvāramityarthaḥ. An interesting explanation is offered for the term sragdharā-dugdharāśiḥ i.e. divya gavya navya dugdhadhārā paramparā. The sragdharā metre employed is a continous stream of fresh milk from the divine cow Kāmadhenu.

As far as the explanation of the verses dealing with Jijñāsādhikaraṇa is concerned, since the Ciñtāmaṇi is quite elucidative our author confines himself to explaining it in the barest terms without being redundant.

The discussion on the parāyanādhikaraṇa affords interesting reading. There are two types of kartṛtva212 - prayojaka and prayojya. For the Lord, śaraṇāgati is only a mere pretext (vyājamātra) and this according to the author may be even a simple act of folding hands (añjalyādyalpa-vyājam). Only the Lord who rules from within as the antaryāmin of all has sarvaprayojakakartṛtva. But since the jīva has received the power of discrimination from the Lord, he has prayojyakartṛtva, but there is no contradiction between the two. In the Cintāmaṇi it is stated that at all times and places the Lord is the motivating factor, but He does this according to one’s past karma. Hence there is no room for any partiality (vaiṣamya) or

[] cruelty (nairghṛṇya) on the part of the Lord toward the jīvas. In the Padayojanā our author declares that the Lord is the witness of the whole universe (sarvalokasākṣī) with its beginningless stream of karma and hence the question of original pravṛtti does not arise (mūlakāraṇa praśnasya nāvakāśaḥ). Towards the end, the author pays his homage to his ācārya and says that he has written this treatise on the lines of Cintāmaṇi (cintāmaṇim anubhāvya).

This is a fairly popular work among the present day scholars. The author supplements and complements the Cintāmaṇi of Śrī Kumāra Varadācārya. The original ideas of Vedāntadeśika are brought out clearly without any ambiguity.

Vādataraṅgiṇī and Other Works

213

This book which is taken up for study, contains the biographical details of the Jīyar rendered in English in about eleven pages followed by its Tamil version which was published earlier in the Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā monthly journal. In pages X and XI, a list of twentysix known works of the Jīyar is given. Of these, only thirteen are available and published in this edition.

[] Another work, Tatkratunyāyasāra214 is available in Manuscript form. This is also taken up for study, in the following pages.

The works studied here are (1) Śrutigītā Vyākhyā (2) Śārīraka Suprabhātam (3) Vādataraṅgiṇī (4) Tārārthasāra (5) Tārārtha-prabhā (6) Tatkratunyāyasāra (7) Sthuṇā-liṅganirṇaya (8) Nyāyasiddhāñjana-kārikā-vimarśanam (9) Ādeśamaṇi (10) Ādeśamaṇi vyākhyā kaustubha (11) Dāsadāsīsamarthanī (12) Makārarakṣā and (13) Sthemadarpaṇa.

According to the editor (p.34) there are still 15 works of the Jīyar which are not available now. They are:- (1) Argalapañcakam (2) Ācārya saptati on his own guru Paruttipaṭṭu Aḻagiya- siṅgar (3) Śrī Ādi Van Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Vaibhava Candrikā (4) Śrī Raṅganātha Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśikastava (5) Āṣāḍhyupākarma-abhyanujñā (6) Gomālā (7) Goṣṭhīnāthastava (8) Yan-prakāśa (9) Yan-prakāśa Vyākhyāna (10) Yatirājabhoga (11) Vedāntakaustubhasāra (12) Vedāntadeśikastavamālikā (13) Raṇapuṅgavastava (14) Śrībhāṣyakāra suprabhātam (15) A few muktaka ślokas.

[]

Śrutigītāvyākhyā

It is a commentary on the 51 verses forming what is called the Śrutigītā in the Bhāgavatapurāṇa, Skandha 10, Chapter 87. This is also known as Vedastuti or Śrīstuti. The mūlaśloka is given first accompanied by a word for word meaning and relevant explanations in prose followed by the author’s śloka that sums up the meaning.

For instance, when explaining śloka 36 (p.41) the author states that the Ācārya is the very incarnation of the Lord and explains the word purupuṇya used in it.

Ācārya-avatara-lakṣaṇa is actually Ācārya-āśrama-viśeṣaṇa-parigrahaṇimittam. In the ensuing śloka, the Lord is addressed as Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha (p.42):

लक्ष्मींनृसिंह तव पादसरोजचिताः पापापहाद्रिसलिला विमदा महान्तः। न्यस्यन्ति ये सकृदिह त्वयि मोक्षकाङ्क्षा- स्ते तीर्थतां दधति गेहसुबल्यजश्च॥ (22)

Towards the end of the commentary, the author states that he had written this work at the behest of the scholars (budhavara) of Tiruvaḷḷūr who were interested to know the Vedic truths contained in the Śruti Gītā.

[]

Śārīraka Suprabhātam

This is a hymn in 32 verses addressed to Lord Devarāja of Kāñcī who is conceived to be (i) reclining on the milky ocean of the wise sayings of teachers like Rāmānuja, and (ii) the Vedic passages explained in the various Adhikaraṇas of the Brahma-sūtras. On the lines of Vedāntadeśika’s Adhikaraṇasārāvalī, the author employs the sragdharā metre and awakens the Lord of Kāñcī.

श्रीरामानुजसूक्तिरुद्धजलधेरूहल्लिं! सुबुद्धितम्।(1)

In v. 20, the author states that the paramapuruṣa is the indweller (antaryāmin) of the whole universe –

तिष्ठत्युञ्चमात्रः पुरुषः इह तथाऽऽङ्गुष्ठमात्रोऽन्तरात्मा लोकानां सन्निविष्टो हृद्य इह सदा भूतभव्येऽशितंति॥(v.20)215

Towards the concluding part of the work the author mentions the year and month in which he composed this stotra –

उदबुद्धा वीरलक्ष्मीशठरिपुणा भिक्षुणा हेमलंम्बि- न्यब्देऽस्मिन् चापमासे दिनगणणगिताः सप्धराः सर्वधार्योः॥ (v. 31)216

Vādataraṅgiṇī

This is a work of grammatical interest in which the author justifies the use of feminine gender in expressions like bhavatīpādalākṣārāsāṅkam

[] (Vedāntadeśika’s Śrīstuti, 4) and bhavati- bhāgadheyena jīvan (Vedāntadeśika’s Haṁsasandeśa, II, 31) where apparently according to Kātyāyana’s Vārtika (V. 3, 28) sarvanāmno vṛttimātre puṁvadbhāvaḥ, the masculine gender has to be used. So, the first example should read as bhavatpādalākṣā etc. As the title suggests, the author advances the arguments and counter arguments in the forms of waves, and concludes that this rule does not apply to the term bhavatī. This work is also mentioned and discussed in another work titled Śiṣṭaprayoga-samīkṣā.217 The author concludes with his salutations to his guru who had blessed him with the philosophical knowledge–

इत्थं पुत्स्वं ब्रुवाणः समिधमसदसोदितस्वादुवादो योऽदाद् वेदान्तविद्बागुरुहृदयसमुल्लासिधीलासिकं मे॥218

Tārārtha Sāra*** and its commentary ***prabhā; Tatkratunyāyasāra:

219

The Tārārtha Sāra is in eight verses and aims to explain the usage of the term eva (एव) in the Śruti text –

तं यथा यथोपासते तथैव भवति।220

[] The author states that the term should be taken to mean ayogavyavaccheda and not anyayogavyavaccheda. This seems to have been a much debated point in scholarly circles during the author’s time. Scholars like Śrī Kṛṣṇa Tātārya Svāmī (better known as Tirupputkuḻi Svāmī), Śrī Kapiṣṭhalam Deśikācārya and Śrīraṅgam Śrīmad Āṇḍavan Svāmī seem to have concurred with the author in this regard; but some of his Tennācārya colleagues disagreed with this viewpoint. Hence the author penned the work Tatkratunyāyasāra, which is an epilogue to his Tārārthasāra and Prabhā.

The Tatkratunyāyasāra is a metrical composition in fifty verses in sragdharā metre similar to the Adhikaraṇasārāvalī of Vedāntadeśika. The style, however, is not as simple as Deśika’s. In this later work, the author sets at rest the opinion of his scholarly colleague who feels that Rāmānuja in his Śrībhāṣya, śarīre bhāvādhikaraṇa (III. 3, 52), meant only the upāsyaguṇa.221 But Vedāntadeśika states in his Rahasyatrayasāra222 that evakāra should be taken to mean the upāsita-guṇas viz. apahatapāpmā, vijara, vimṛtyu, viśoka, vijighatsa, apipāsa, satyakāma and satyasaṅkalpa. The Chāndogya text (III,14,7) reads as follows:

यथाक्रतुरस्मिन् लोके पुरुषो भवति तथेतः प्रेत्य भवति।

[] The pratyagātmā is upāsyabhūta parabrahma śarīra and hence the qualities that are meditated upon will be acquired later, but not to the exclusion of the other qualities that are natural to the jīva.

उपासितानां गुणादेर्यां प्राप्तावप्यबहिष्क्रिया। सा तत्क्रतुनयग्राह्या नाकारान्तवर्जनम्॥223

The author refers to his work Tārārthasāra in vv. 2, 17, 30, 46 and 50.

The following śloka v. 11 elucidates the author’s stand:

मुक्ते नभस्युदस्तात् क्रतुनयबलातोऽपि युदस्तान् बभाषे तस्मिद्भिः तत्क्रतुर्केरितानिरवनाथत्यपादात्र साध्या। उक्ता योग्यामुदासे प्रकृतिरहितचिन्मात्रमुख्यत्वभाजे प्राप्तौ ब्रह्मानवाप्यं क्रतुनयनिमाज्जिह्वलेप्यत्वभङ्गात्॥ … एवं श्रुत्या तु या या फलति परमृतिः साऽपरोपास्यनूना- कारित्वेत्यथसिद्धिः क्वचिदसति परोपासने न्यूनता का॥ (12)

Hence only the śuddharūpāṣṭaka jīva has to be contemplated upon. The eight qualities of the jīva are contextual and are neither redundant nor synonymous. There is variety of choice in the qualities to be contemplated depending on the desire of the devotee (upāsaka). The author quotes time and again from the Śrībhāṣya and Vedāntadeśika’s Tattvatīkā to prove his point that the jīva has to be contemplated only as phalagavisayātirūpa. i.e., as pure (amala). He refutes the views of Naiyāyikas (vv. 36 and 39 - pāṣāṇakalpa mokṣa). According to one alternative, is jñātṛtva natural to the jīva, or is it

[] what one experiences like prāṇa (v. 38)? But this jñātṛtva is the result of indriyas (v. 43) and hence this alternative has to be rejected. In v. 39 the author states that pūrvapakṣins have only gained in unnecessarily creating an apparent discord between Rāmānuja and Vedāntadeśika (muniyugakalaha). So, the author categorically states in v. 42 :

तस्मा ज्ज्वरीभावधिकाणफलितं निर्मलात्मरूपे ध्येयत्वं नो विशिष्यापहतदुरितलादिरूपे तथालम्।

In vv. 44,45 and 46 he again states that the interpretation of anyayogavyavaccheda ascribed to Rāmānuja is not correct. He states in v. 48 that those who meditate on the pañcāgni-vidyā also attain liberation even like those who perform any other upāsanā and become equal in all respects to the Lord with the exception of lokavyāpāra. He raises a pertinent question as to whether after attaining liberation, upāsanā comes to an end, but answers immediately that upāsanā continues even in the state of mokṣa in the form of kaiṅkarya to Bhagavān and Bhāgavatas. In the concluding verse, the author states that he has written this work in Śrīperumbūdūr having sipped (mastered) the essence of Tatkratunyāyasāra from his earlier ācāryas (cuḷukitam).

The verse numbers given here are in accordance with the numbers given in the available manuscript. However the first three verses and the last two verses are not numbered in the manuscript. This work brings to light the

[] great veneration the author had for his ācāryas and his incapacity to bear any misinterpretations of their works or any divergence in their views.

Sthūṇāliṅgavinirṇayaḥ

This is a poetical piece in 16 verses justifying the usuage of the word sthūṇā in feminine gender by Vedāntadeśika in his Daśāvatārastotra (v.5).

या काचित् सहसा महासुरगृहस्थूणा पितामह्यभूत्

According to the critic, the word sthūṇa is only neuter in gender. But in the Daśāvatāravyākhyāna,224 two explanations are given: (a) the word sthūṇā added to the single word gṛha becomes neuter by the sūtra, gṛha-śaśābhyam klībe (b) In the sūtra (I-22) gṛha-śaśābhyam klībe it is stated that though gṛhasthūṇa is nityanapuṁsaka, according to śalākā strīyāṁ nityam (I-34) the word nithya indicates optionality of the femine gender elsewhere. The relevant verse 10 reads as follows:225

यत्र स्थूणाया प्राग् गृहपदघटना स्यात् तदा क्लीबतैव प्राग्न्नं स्वामिशब्दात्परपदघटितं प्राक्पुरं स्युपयोगि। पश्चात् स्वाम्यन्वये तु प्रकृतगृहपदार्थान्वयः पारिषिकः स्यात् स्थूणो साक्षाच्च पल्युं न स भवति ततः सुष्ठु नः सर्वसिद्धिः॥

[] Concluding the work he states that he has written this in Tiruvahindrapuram and pays his salutations to Vedāntadeśika –

जयति निगमचूडार्यशिष्यदिव्यजनम्यनुतिः। जयति रमानृगराड्यसुरगृहस्थूणायशस्स्तंजननीकः॥

Nyāyasiddhāñjanakārikāvimarsanam

This work in 19 verses elucidates the meaning contained in the following śloka occuring in the Jīvapariccheda of Vedānta Deśika’s Nyāyasiddhāñjana:

इयं केवललक्ष्मीशोपायत्वप्रख्यातिरूपिका। स्वहेतुत्वधियं रुन्धे किं पुनः सहकारिणाम्॥

In this track, the author criticises the interpretation offered to this kārikā by some, to the effect that surrender (śaraṇāgati) cannot be the bestower of all the desired fruits. Referring to Kūreśa’s Varadarājastava (v. 88) he explains that the term sva in the kārikā should be taken to mean prapannāya and not prapattaye. The relevant verse in the Jīyar’s vimarśanam reads as under:-

इत्थं चात्र स्वशब्दः परिमृशति पराधीनकर्तृप्रपन्नं स्वाधीनश्रीशताम्प्राप्त्यहमवधरणं तुल्यमेवोपहन्यात्। वैधी न्यासात्मधीस्तु स्वयतनविघिप्राप्तिकेत्युक्तवत् यत् तद्रूपस्वोपरोधं वदति रुधिरय ज्रीर्हि सङ्कल्पगर्भा॥

[] That prapatti cannot be the bestower of fruits is negated by the author in v. 19, wherein he states that the very act of surrender is not merely the surrender of body and soul, but the surrender of even one’s knowledge that one is the agent of any action. So, this total self-effacement itself is capable of generating all fruits (akhilaphala).

The author concludes by stating that he has written this commentary in Śrīperumbūdūr and prays that Lord Nṛsiṁha be pleased with his work.

Ādeśamaṇi with commentary Kaustubha

This work in 38 sragdharā verses explains the formation of the word ādeśa found in the Chāndogya passage uta tamādeśamaprākṣayaḥ. Advaitins derive the word ādeśa by adding the suffix ghañ to the root diś in the sense of the object (karmaṇi) or the instrument (karaṇa). Thus according to them ādeśaḥ means that which is arrived at through instruction (upadeśagamyaḥ) or a sentence which conveys an instruction (upadeśātmakam vākyam).

But, Śrī Rāmānuja takes this word in the sense of ‘one who commands’ in the lines of the Śruti sentences - antaḥ praviṣṭaḥ śāsthā janānām226 and praśāsitāram sarveṣām227. It follows that the word ādeśaḥ according to Rāmānuja is derived from the root diś by adding the suffix ghañ in the sense

[] of agent (kartari) by applying the rule, padarujaviśaspṛśo ghañ etc., through yogavibhāga.

The present work Ādeśamaṇi along with its commentary Kaustubha was written to refute the derivations given to the word ādeśa by one Bhāradvāja Raṅgācārya in his work Ādeśakaumudī.228 The author had earlier written a work Gomālā (which is not extant now) to refute the work Ādeśakaumudī. But, later because it was rather difficult to be comprehended by the less learned, the author took upon himself the task of writing the above work with its commentary. The author’s scholarship and erudition are well reflected in this presentation. His mastery of Sanskrit grammar is also well marked. His interest to interpret the correct meanings intended by earlier ācāryas and his large-heartedness in writing in simpler language so as to cover larger audience is certainly to be commended.

Dāsadāsīsamarthanī

This work is in 10 verses, justifying the expression tvaddāsadāsīgaṇaḥ found in v. 1 of Yāmuna’s Catuślokī. A few objections were raised by the critics to the effect that – a) there should be ekaśeṣa compound according to Pāṇini’s rule. pumān striyā (I-2-67) and

[] b) the usage should be dāsīdāsam according to gaṇapāṭha of the sūtra “gavāśvaprabhṛtīni”(II.4-11). The author justifies the usuage of the compound dāsadāsī according to Pāṇini’s grammatical rules to the effect that ekaśeṣa is not compulsory. The relevant v. 6 is quoted below.

किञ्चाश्रेयत्यधीतत्वन्नियमव्यावर्त्यरूपं गणे दासीदासपदेऽपि तुल्यनयतो वाच्यं हि तस्सिद्धये। इन्द्रादिं प्रतिलोमरूपमतुलन्यायाश्रयेद् अन्यथा सद्बोधो नियमस्य वाच्य इति तद् द्वन्द्वोऽत्र भाष्यज्ञपः॥

The author refers to the Śrībhāṣya and Catuślokī Vyākhyāna of Śrī Vedāntadeśika to prove his point. The author’s mastery of Sanskrit grammar and his interest to convey the correct meaning intended by Rāmānuja and Yāmuna is perceptible. The author concludes by paying his obeisance to Yāmunayogivarya and Rāmānuja.

Makārarakṣā

This work is also in 10 verses, and explains the last syllable m of the *praṇava “om” which according to the Śrīvaiṣṇava Ācāryas refers to the individual soul (jīva). In the Mūlamantrādhikāra of Śrīmad Rahasya-trayasāra, Vedāntadeśika explains that the term “ma” is derived from the root “man” in the sense of “to know”- mana jñāne: mān māne (to measure) māsi parimāṇe (to measure) manu avabodhane (to understand). In all these instances, m alone remains after effecting the tilopa.

[] Now the objection is to the usage of makāra at the end of the word instead of nakāra by the grammatical rule mo no dhātoḥ (VIII.2. 64). The present track tries to answer the objections by resorting to the rule uṇādayo bahulam (III. 3. 1). The author states that this objection can be refuted even by resorting to the rule - chandasi dṛṣṭānuvidhiḥ and objection can be brushed aside by saying that it is cāndhasaprayoga. But the author’s intention was to answer the objection in the traditional manner, grammatically, and hence the necessity of writing this piece. Once again, the author’s proficiency in Sanskrit grammar is well-established.

Sthemadarpaṇaḥ

This work in prose is devoted to justify the usuage of the word sthema found in the opening verse of the Śrībhāṣya –

अखिलभुवनजन्मस्थेमभङ्गादिलिेले

According to some critics sthema is ungrammatical. As a matter of fact, the word sthema was used by Vācaspatimiśra in his commentary Vidhiviveka on Nyāyakaṇikā thus - bhuvanabhavanasthemadhvaṁsa etc. Murāri in his Anargharāghava (VI. 36) also used it niḥsthemāno daśānanakīrtayaḥ in the sense of ‘maintenance’.

But the author takes recourse to grammar, and justifies the usage of the word sthema

[] by a) deriving it from the word sthira, b) resorting to the word sthāma to which a suffix is added and c) by adding the suffix imanic to the verb sthā under the rule uṇādayo bahulam.

The author concludes this treatise by paying his homage to Rāmānuja, Patañjali and Vedāntadeśika using alluring alliterative style.

पतञ्जली नित्यकृताञ्जलिः स्यामपाकृतक्वान्तवाचोदोषि। योगेन शब्देन च वैष्णकेन प्रेम्णा भुवि स्थेमकृति द्विजिह्वै॥

जयति निगमान्तधूरिर्वतिपतिवाणी सुधोरणी जयति। जयति फणितिः पुराणी सर्वमस्येमधारीणी जयति॥

The concluding verse reads as under –

गोपालसूर्यशिष्यसोभानमेधापरिखराद्विराजिताम्। स्थेमाधिवादपदमर्पणेन जीवाचिरं श्रीनृहरेः पदाब्जे॥

In the appendix, the Svāmī’s vaibhava containing biographical details and his composition is presented in 30 ślokas.

In the foregoing pages, a brief analysis has been attempted of the work of Piḷḷaippākkam Svāmī, His Holiness the 37th pontiff of the Śrī Ahobila Maṭha. The depth of meaning enshrined in his works, his mastery over the Sanskrit grammar, his well-grounded erudition and scholarship and his devotion to ācārya sūktis are evident in his compositions.

[] The above study affords a glimpse into the philosophical and literary contributions of the Jīyars of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha to the cause of Śrīvaiṣṇavism. This selective study, it is hoped, will generate an interest into making an indepth study of the several individual works of the Jīyars.

[]

CONCLUSION

In the foregoing chapters, the contribution of the Jīyars of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha has been studied from the view-points of philosophy, religion, literature and society. In fact all these constitute different facets of an integral whole. To the man of intellect, the philosophical interpretations and the commentaries on the grantha-catuṣṭaya afford food for thought; to the man of religion, the arcā worship of Śrī Mālola Nṛsiṁha, the dolotsavas of Lord Kṛṣṇa conducted at various individual residences and the daily religious schedule of the pontifical head, afford a veritable feast to the eyes and heart alike; to the man of letters, the “Mālola Vidvat Sadas” (scholarly assemblies) conducted annually, as also during special occasions, the arguments and counter-arguments put forth by erudite scholars and the Jīyar ’s samādhānams are a treat to the ears and mind alike: and, to the society at large, the head of the Ahobila monastic order is not a total recluse, but very much a society-personage, involved in the rejuvenation of society. The Jīyar ’s heart goes out to the suffering humanity and he is ever ready, like the Lord Himself, to alleviate their suffering by teaching them the easiest way to salvation viz., surrender (śaraṇāgati) which requires the mammoth mental attitude viz., supreme confidence (mahāviśvāsa) in the salvic power of the Lord.

The Jīyar is also well aware of the current affairs of the state and the country as is evidenced by his grant of Rs. 50,000 to the Andhra Pradesh

[] Chief Minister’s Cyclone Relief Fund on November 20th 1996.229 Further, the Jīyar has aslo asked all the branches of the Ahobila Maṭha in the country and also his disciples to provide succour to the suffering. This indeed is the very spirit of Śrīvaiṣṇavism namely, the inability to bear the suffering of others (paraduḥkha asahiṣṇutva).

By leading an exemplary life of detatched attachment, performing his daily and ordained duties, the pontifical head is setting standards to humanity and is showing the way in which to lead one’s life according to one’s varṇa and āśrama. By performing pañcasaṁskāras and accepting bharanyāsa, he is discharging his duty ordained by Lord Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha Himself and is thus, carrying on the six-century old tradition commencing from Śrī Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa, the first Jīyar.

The Jīyar lends a helping hand in the renovation of old and dilapidated temples (punaruddhāraṇa) seeing to it that there is no cessation of daily worship to the arcā idols in these temples. Donations in the form of valuable ornaments to the presiding deities in many temples are also made. To cite a recent instance, on the eve of the Jīyar ’s 70th birthday (November 22, 1996), a diamond studded “Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha Padakam” costing about

[] Rs. 2 lakhs was donated to the temple authorities at Tirumala to adorn the presiding deity, Lord Śrīnivāsa.

Further, the Maṭha plays an important role in the training of the temple priests (arcakas) and in educating them to be cultured and devoted in their service to the Lord. Vedic knowledge must be made available to the young and love for the highest moral virtues must be cultivated. From this view-point, the contribution of the Jīyar of Iñjimeḍu in setting up a Vedapāṭhaśālā at Madhurāntakam is very significant; and up to this day, it is fulfilling the purpose creditably. Students are enrolled at a very tender age and are imparted the study of Vedas and Divyaprabandham alike. This is how the Ubhayavedānta tradition is carried down the centuries.

The Maṭha provides sat-saṅga, i.e. company of practising spiritual personalities who have perfected their lives in the light of the Divine inflow and persence. To be in their company is to undergo a transformation as it were. Thus, the Maṭha offers opportunities for the making of the saints through sat-sampradāya i.e. holy presence and worship and caters to the demands of all persons who are anxious to have the blessings of a spiritual life and opportunities of receiving the love of God in abundance.

As a matter of fact, the basic priniciples of one’s religion should not be surrendered but should be restated, with a special reference to the needs of a more complex and changing social order. It is in this context that the Maṭha is

[] becoming more and more relevant to modern times. Religion is often misunderstood to be the outer practices, routine rituals and creeds unique to a particular culture. Even those who attempt to understand and follow their religion often get confused because they find it difficult to rationalise the beliefs and practices. Religious plurality is an undeniable fact and harmony should be the goal of all religions. Mankind today stands at the cross-roads of civilization, where on the one hand man is reaping the fruits of science and technology ushered in by the Industrial Revolution and on the other, he is searching for inner peace by tracing his cultural roots through religion. India has had a long cultural tradition and perhaps one of the earliest civilizations, the Vedic had integrated science and religion with success. In this mobile social order when there is inter-cultural rapport between continents, it is imperative that the enlightened pontifical heads initiate measures to educate the masses by creating awareness about the time-tested Vedic injunctious such as satyam vada, dharmam cara and so on, and focus on the grave consequences of neglecting these values which are essential for the very survival of mankind. The younger generation has to be taught that there is an inextricable connection between science and religion, though their methods are antithetical i.e. reason versus revelation.

All our Vedānta schools accept logic and their philosophies are substantiated and tempered by reason. It has now become imperative that the subtle concepts of Vedānta be communicated in a way relevant to the modern

[] generation. Our concept of Sanātana Dharma is scientific and has withstood the test of time. Though the need for religious harmony has been proclaimed by the enlightened in every generation, religious differences continue to create discord within society and between nations. To root out this problem and foster religious tolerence, fanaticism should be eradicated. The world would lose its vitality if one creed absorbed another. Variety is the spice of life and hence a rich harmony and not colourless uniformity is the ideal to be aimed for. Intellectualism has a tendency to separate the dissimilar and shut them up in different compartments, but , higher intuition takes note of the natural differences in all spheres and seeks to combine them into an integrated whole. S. Radhakrishnan230 states “The half-religious and the irreligious fight about dogmas and not the truly religious…. and the more religious we grow, the more tolerant of diversity shall we become”. Hence it is that a pontifical head is ideally suited to achieve this spirit of unity in diversity (each fulfilling its function and all directed by the force of the Divine to achieve the most exquisite harmony). It is indeed by performing this duty that a Maṭhādhipati can fulfill the heartfelt desire of Vedāntadeśika –

परस्परहितैषिणां परिसरेषु मां वर्तय।231 “Let me reside in the vicinity of those who desire mutual welfare”.

[] Any research is not an end in itself. Infact it should be a beginning and should open up new vistas for further research and study. In this aspect, the present study, it is hoped, can encourage comparative study of the other Śrīvaiṣṇava Maṭhas, a study of the contribution of Piḷḷaippākkam Jīyar (37th Pontiff) to Śrīvaiṣṇavism and the preparation of a Critical Edition of Śrī Nārāyaṇa Muni’s Rahasyatrayajīvātu. Efforts should also be made by aspiring young scholars to trace some of the valuable works of the Pontiffs which are mere names to us.

  1. Jawaharlal Nehru, The Discovery of India, pp. 61-62, Asia Publishing House, New Delhi, 1961. [] The Śrīvaiṣṇava system of thought primarily implies the inseparability of Śrī and Viṣṇu. According to this system, Lord Viṣṇu is the Supreme Being and He constitutes the Godhead, along with His divine consort Goddess Lakṣmī - “the Śrī par excellence”. Retaining their individual characteristics they supplement and complement each other in their līlā – ↩︎

  2. The famous words of Portia in Shakespeare’s Merchant of Venice, wherein this quality of ‘mercy’ is extolled to heavenly heights (Act IV, Sc. l), p. 246. [] With this we enter a new movement called the Āḻvār movement which was to have a lasting influence in the growth of Śrīvaiṣṇavism especially in South India. The great feature of the Āḻvār movement was that it was more emotional than metaphysical. “Āḻvār” in Tamil means “one who is immersed”. These saints were so called because they were always immersed in their love and devotion to Lord Viṣṇu. The Āḻvārs were uncompromsing in their love for and worship of Viṣṇu. The hymns of the Āḻvārs were the outbursts of God-filled souls for whom God was an intimate personal friend and guide. ↩︎

  21. We are not given any details regarding the date or name of the King. [] installation of Vedāntadeśika’s image in the temple precincts22 and the renovation of the temple Gopuram. The holy trinity of Śrīvaiṣṇavite worship and pilgrim centres: (a) the Periya Kovil at Śrīraṅgam, (b) Perumāḷ Kovil at Kāñcī and (c) Tiruvenkaṭam - Tirumala, must have demanded the Jīyar’s attention. He ordered the construction of the Sopāna (steps) leading to Tirumalai Hill. Several rest houses and maṭhas were constructed on the wayside and arrangements were made for their maintenance. Evidence of the Jīyar’s influence and of these permanent arrangements can be seen in the Tirumala Tirupati Inscriptions of 1516, 1546 and 1556 A.D. These Inscriptions along with the Ahobilam Inscriptions go to prove the Jīyar’s organisational capabilities. Enough to say that the influence of the Maṭha at Tirumala abides to this day; such is the strength of the foundation laid by this Jīyar. ↩︎

  22. Refer Commemoration Volume published on the occasion of the Silver Jubilee of the coronation of H.H. the 44th Aḻagiya Siṅgar. Śrī Rāmānuja is believed to have been the incarnation of Ādiśeṣa who appeared as a five-hooded serpent and nodded approval during the Jīyar’s exposition of Śrī Rāmānuja’s Śrībhāṣya. This led to the second event namely, the punarnirmāṇa of the gopuram at Tirunārāyaṇapuram and installation of Vedānta at Āḻvār Tirunagari. [] seen in a pillar of the maṇṭapam, wherein is sculptured Lord Nṛsiṁha and facing the Lord is sculptured Śrī Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Svāmī in another pillar. He stayed for sometime here and gave discourses on Grantha-catuṣṭayam viz. the Śrībhāṣya, the Gītābhāṣya, the Bhagavad-viṣayam and the Śrīmad Rahasyatrayasāra. Here he chanced to meet Udayabhānumiśra, a rich Advaitin from Bengal. This meeting resulted in a challenge to the superiority of the rival systems. The discussions started as usual with the preliminary wager of the loser embracing the religion of the winner. The Jīyar took recourse to the Śatadūṣaṇī of Vedāntadeśika and defeated his opponent. Udayabhānumiśra surrendered at the Jīyar’s feet and embraced Śrīvaiṣṇavism, had his pañca-saṁskāra at the Jīyar’s hands and offered all his wealth at the Jīyar’s feet. This wealth was utilised for the construction of buildings at Kāñ-ci and a separate temple for Vedāntadeśika, maṇṭapas etc. However, nothing more is heard of this Udayabhānumiśra. ↩︎

He spent the rest of his life giving discourses to his devotees on the Śrīvaiṣṇava religion and philosophy. ↩︎

  41. part I, appendix, p.1. [] The Supreme Being (Brahman) is only One (Advaita). But It has all the animate and inanimate objects (cit and acit) as Its attributes. Though there is thus mutual difference between the modes and the possessor (Brahman) and between the modes themselves, the whole thing is conceived of as a complex three-in-one single unit. This concept that the entire world of men and matter form the body of the one and only supreme Brahman is the basis of Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy.   53. Ibid. p.3. [] “Asau Asau” refers to a distant object ultimately denoting Brahman. “Asau Asau” also means that each and every Ācārya’s Holy Name (Tiru Nāma) should be recited separately and hence this practice of reciting the taniyans (dedicatory stanzas) of Ācāryas individually. Teachers of right mind are described as those who are free from egotism (nirahaṅkārī), desiring others’ welfare (parasamṛddhipriyaḥ), not attracted by love of fame or wealth (khyātilābhanirapekṣa). The compassionate guru leads the suffering soul to the Lord taking upon himself the burden of sin invested by the mumukṣu as nyāsa. This is bharanyāsa or bharasamarpaṇa. ↩︎

[] these works is available now, though quotations from the latter are made by Vedāntadeśika “the most brilliant luminary in the history of the post-Rāmānuja Śrīvaiṣṇavism”.3 From the references made to the Nyāyatattva, it appears to have been the first work in the field to interpret Nyāya according to the Śrīvaiṣṇava line of thought. It criticises and controverts the Nyāyasūtras of Gautama. Vedāntadeśika quotes several metrical and prose passages from it in his Nyāyasiddhāñjana and Nyāyapariśuddhi. The system of Viśiṣṭādvaita, as Vedāntadeśika observes in his Tattvamuktākalāpa, owes its origin to Nāthamuni and was developed and nurtured by Yāmuna his grandson, and protected and kept on a firm footing by Rāmānuja. ↩︎

[] (b) the Stotraratna which in sixty-five verses praises Lakṣmīpati (Lord Viṣṇu), hints at the secret doctrine of self-surrender and highlights the ācāryabhakti of the author, (c) the Gītārthasaṅgraha which in thirty-two verses expounds in brief, the traditional teachings of the Gītā, (d) the Āgamaprāmāṇya which establishes the authority of the Pāñcarātra texts, (e) the Siddhitraya (Ātma-, Īśvara-, Saṁvit- siddhis) which depicts the nature of the individual soul and the superme Lord and their relation to each other. In the last section he refutes the doctrine of avidyā and Monism of Śaṅkara, (f) the Mahāpuruṣanirṇaya which is not available now, establishes the supremacy of Lord Nārāyaṇa over other deities on the strength of various authoritative texts (mainly the Upaniṣads) reinforced by reasonings.4 ↩︎

  60. Vide V. Rangacharya, op.cit. p. 174. [] said to have lived for 120 years. Yāmuna died before Rāmānuja became an Ācārya, and the interval was filled up by two scholars, Mahāpūrṇa and Śrīśailapūrṇa. ↩︎

  61. N. Jagadeesan, History of Śrī Vaiṣṇavism in the Tamil Country (Post-Rāmānuja), p. 47, Koodal Publishers, Madurai, 1977. [] It should be pointed out in the true spirit of Śrīvaiṣṇavism that this emergence of two principal sects was only a natural evolution. A true Śrīvaiṣṇavite instead of condemning the rift and the resulting tension between the two sects, should appreciate the positive aspect of this division. To the true devotee of Śrīvaiṣṇavism deeply rooted in South India, the Vaḍakalai sect constitutes the secondary root spreading all over the northern part of Tamil country and the Teṅgalai sect constitutes the secondary root spreading across the southern part of the Tamil country viz. the Kāveri delta centering around Śrīraṅgam. To a student of Śrīvaiṣṇavism this diversification, without secession from the mainstream of Śrīvaiṣṇavism, provides a treasure-house of invaluable literature which otherwise would never have been penned at all. It stands testimony to the “unity in diversity” principle incorporated in Śrīvaiṣṇavism. It is moreover indicative of the sūkṣmabuddhi (subtle intellect) of pūrvācāryas like Yāmuna and Rāmānuja whose philosophical works were capable of variegated interpretations, intelligent and thought-provoking in their own ways. It goes to the credit of later ācāryas like Vedāntadeśika and Maṇavāḷa Māmuṉi to have enriched the Śrīvaiṣṇava literary world with their innumerable commentaries. A true Śrīvaiṣṇavite, hence will not bemoan the split, if one may call it, but will thank his ācāryas for having provided valuable food for his thought. [] The standard differences between the Vaḍakalais and the Teṅgalais are usually enumerated as eighteen and are termed aṣṭādaśa bhedas. One of the main points of dispute is the importance which each sect attaches to Sanskrit and Tamil languages. While the Vaḍakalais give priority to the chanting of Vedas in temples, the Teṅgalais give preference to the recitation of Tamil prabandhams. Prapatti is a philosophical point which divides Vaḍakalais and Teṅgalais. The word “prapatti” means “an approach to God”. For the Vaḍakalais “prapatti” is qualified by certain conditions: a) acquisition of qualities which would make one fit for offering to God; b) avoidance of conduct not acceptable to God; c) faith that God alone is the protector; d) appeal for protection; e) a feeling of helplessness; and f) absolute surrender. ↩︎

  62. Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā, 37- 28,29. ↩︎

  63. Lakṣmī Tantra, Adyar Library Series, 17-74. [] performed even in the beginning i.e. without attempting other ways to salvation like jñāna and bhakti yogas. The Vaḍakalai point of view is that prapatti is to be attempted only as a last resort, after failing in karma, jñāna and bhakti yogas. These differences in turn leads to the question of whether Divine grace is set in motion by human effort or not i.e. whether it is sahetuka or nirhetuka. This again fringes on the question as to whether man has to expiate for his sins and then obtain Divine Grace, or whether Divine Grace being irresistible, compassion and grace are showered on erring souls, ignoring their faults or even delighting in their sins because it provides God with an opportunity for showering His Grace. This situation is somewhat eased by introducing “Śrī” as the puruṣakāra or Mediator, to intervene for mercy on behalf of erring souls to the Ever just and Lawful Lord. Her role as Divine Mother is accomplished by Her essential characteristic of compassion and for the Vaḍakalais Śrī is puruṣakāra; She is the upāya (means) even like Śrīman Nārāyaṇa for attaining salvation and She is also upeya (goal) like Lord Nārāyaṇa in Vaikuṇṭha attended upon by ever-liberated souls (nityasūris) and other liberated souls. She is the paramātmā like Nārāyaṇa and is not jīvātmā. She is also vibhu (all-pervasive) like Nārāyaṇa and can grant liberation (mokṣa). The Teṅgalais accept Her role as mediator and agree that She is a means for liberation. But they disagree in that She is a jīvātmā and is finite (aṇu) in nature. [] There is another point of difference regarding kaivalya which consists in self-realisation. While the Teṅgalais consider those who have reached kaivalya as liberated, the Vaḍagalais maintain that kaivalya is inferior to paramapada and is situated outside Vaikuṇṭha. There are also differences regarding the interpretation of the caramaśloka (Gītā, xviii, 66). The phrase “sarvadharmān parityajya” is interpreted by Vaḍakalais to mean those who are in capable of performing bhakti yoga but the Teṅgalais interpret it as “having to give up all duties” before seeking refuge in the Lord. Post-prapatti sins are to be atoned for afresh by Vaḍakalais but for the Teṅgalais, prapatti once done holds good forever. One other point of difference relates to the performance of one’s daily duties (nityakarma) like sandhyāvandana etc.; for the Teṅgalais a prapanna need not perform these duties. ↩︎

Can a mere acetana bamboo tridaṇḍa afford protection? Obviously no. It is the viṣṇusvarūpa within that protects. ↩︎

  98. History of Sri Vaishnavism in the Tamil Country (Post - Rāmānuja), p. 149. [] Dharma for proselytisation and so there were no institutions for that purpose. The business of the Hindu Maṭhas was rather to conserve and save, regulate, guide and consolidate, than to convert and expand. The numerous discussions which the religious leaders of these institutions held with the leader of the heretical sects were not attempts so much to spread Hinduism as to destroy heresy. So, the Maṭhas were a sort of new defence system to preserve Hinduism. ↩︎

  105. Ghaṭikāśatam Varadācārya - great grandson of Naḍādūr Ammāḷ (Vātsya Varadācārya) was known to be a prolific composer capable of composing a hundred verses in a ghaṭikā (Nāḻigai - 24 minutes). He was in charge of a ghaṭikā (centre of learning) in Kāñcī after his Ācārya, Brahma Tantra Svatantra Jīyar - I. The Ghaṭikā drew many illustrious learners. (History of Śrīvaiṣṇavism in the Tamil Country - [Post-Rāmānuja], p.134.) [] appeared before him in the guise of an ascetic and Himself bestowed the robes of a saṁnyāsin on Śrīnivāsa,14 administered in his ears the preṣamantra, ordained him into the ascetic order, offered him the tridaṇḍa and called him Śrī Śaṭhakopa Jīyar. The Lord revealed to him His real Self and nominated him to be His Ārādhaka, thenceforth bestowed the Śaṅkha and Cakra from His Divine hands and made this clear command: “Worshipping me, go to villages and towns, grant aspirants the sacred symbols, mantras and their significance. Accept their surrender and proceed to sustain the way of Viśiṣṭādvaita as expounded by Āḻvārs, Rāmānuja and Vedāntadeśika.”15 The Jīyar thus received the charter of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha from its progenitor Lord Nṛsiṁha Himself. This happened on the fifth day of the bright fortnight (Śuklapakṣapañcamī) Puraṭṭāci month, Bahudhānya year (in the Śaka year 1301), exactly on his twentieth birthday (tīrunakṣatra) – September 18, 1379 A.D. He stayed on in Ahobilam and started an organisation with a band of people possessing missionary zeal, called Ahobila Dāsaris who worked for the uplift of the hill tribes (ceñcus) living in and around Ahobilam.16 An idol of Lord ↩︎

  108. The Ahobilam Inscription of Śrīraṅga Rāya dated 1584-85 says that Śrī Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Svāmi III, the 7th Jīyar of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha who had installed the idol of Śrīvaiṣṇava Āḻvār at Jagannath approached King Śrīraṅga Rāya and asked for the restoration of the District to its original condition by recovering it from Muslims. From the Inscription it seems that one Mukunda Harichandra of Orissa was a contemporary of this Jīyar. The idea of the installation of Āḻvār idols at Pūrī is corroborated by the play Vāsantikā Pariṇayam (see S. Krishnaswamy Iyengar, Sources of Vijayanagar History, pp. 233-234). This takes the 7th Jīyar to the end of the 16th century A.D. B.V. Rāmānujam believes in the contemporaneity of Ṣaṣṭha Parāṅkuśa (6th Jīyar) and Aliya Rāma Rāya and Sadāśiva Rāya on the strength of the Satsampradāya Muktāvali. As these arguments are fairly conclusive, the traditional dates assigned to some of the Jīyars may have to be post-dated. But S.N. Venkatesa Iyer has “thought it best to stick to the Sri Sannidhi Guru Paramparā as the safest course with our present imperfect knowledge of the times” (History of Ahobila Maṭha, p.19). [] The Guruparamparā then takes us from Orrisa to the capital of the Karnataka kingdom, presumably Vijayanagar, where the Jīyar stayed for sometime enjoying the patronage of the local king.21 It states that the Jīyar met one Maṇalpākkam Toḷappar who learnt all the Śāstras from the Jīyar. Impressed by Toḷappar’s quick mastery of all Śāstras, the Jīyar asked him to write dharma śāstra digests for Śrīvaiṣṇavas. In accordance with the Jīyar’s wish he wrote eight works - Gṛhyaratnam, Kaṇṭhabhūṣaṇam, Smṛtiratnākara, Pitṛmedhasāra, and its commentary Sudhīvilocanam, Daśanirṇaya, Āśaucaśatakam, and Āśaucanirṇaya. The Jiyar having no other option, admitted him into the ascetic order and nominated him as the next pontiff, with the stipulation that the practices pertaining to both the traditions be followed in the Maṭha”. However, no such reference is found in the Śrī Sannidhi Guruparamparā. According to T.A. Swaminatha Aiyar (Ācārya Carittira Mālā - Appendix), the first eleven chiefs of the Maṭha (Parakāla Maṭha) were subject to Śrī Ahobila Maṭha because the Parakāla Maṭha tradition includes the recitation of certain Taniyans peculiar to the Ahobila Maṭha.   130. Ācārya Vaibhavam, p. 274, Śrī Viśiṣṭādvaita Research Centre, 1992. See also “The Hindu” 13th November, 1966. [] The Jīyar proceeded in his religious tour to North India. He visited Badarī34 and then returned via Tirumala to South India. He visited Madhuramaṅgalam, Nīrvalūr, Iñjimeḍu, Kaḷathūr, Uragaḍam and Kāñcī and then proceeded to Ahobilam. During his pontificate, the Maṭha became a wealthy and powerful institution. The Jīyar himself enjoyed the status of a rājasaṁnyāsī and was provided with a paraphernalia consisting of elephants, horses, guards, etc., The Jīyar was assisted in his pontifical duties by one Rāmānuja Aiyaṅgār, who was his Mudrādhikārī. The Śrī Sannidhi Guruparamparā states that this Jīyar had a divine call from Jvālā Nṛsiṁha to worship the Lord permanently in His cave. Hence at the Lord’s command he initiated Iḷaṅkāḍu Kavisārvabhauma Śikhāmaṇi into asceticism and appointed him as his successor and entered the Holy cave in Aṅgirasa year, Māsi month, Kṛṣṇapakṣa pañcamī tithi (February 1513).35 It is stated that the Jīyar has authored the following works - Siddhānta Maṇi Dīpa, Pañcakāla Dīpikā, Prapatti Prayoga, and Nṛsiṁha Stava. He ruled the pontificate for fourteen years and one month from January 1499 to February 1513. ↩︎

  152. There was a gap of about two years before a worthy successor could be found by the king of Gadvāl. [] was Śrī Vānamāmālācārī. This Svāmī and his younger brother Śrī Veṅkaṭanārāyaṇācār Svāmī were well known scholars in their village. So excellent were their memory skills, that they could discourse on the Śrībhāṣya, Śrutaprakāśikā and Īḍu Muppattāṟāyirappaṭi without referring to the concerned books. Both of them were well-grounded in jñāna and anuṣṭhāna. ↩︎

  154. See Satsampradāya muktāvali, p. 162. [] He proceeded from Nṛsiṁhapuram to Ādanūr, Pullambūtaṅguḍi, Kumbhakoṇam, Kāñcī and Tiruvaḷḷūr.   157. Ācārya vaibhavam, p. 333. [] after the Jīyar performed the dolotsava to Lord Kṛṣṇa. To this, the dacoit leader agreed and even while the maṅgalārati was being performed to the Lord, the dacoit leader and his gang shouted and wailed as if in great pain. The Svāmī then ordered the holy water to be sprinkled on the dacoits. Only after this, they were pacified and it came to be known through the dacoits leader that during the pūjā they seemed to be attacked by hordes of lions and the holy water drove them away. The dacoits then fell at the Jīyar’s feet begging for forgiveness. ↩︎

  161. There was a legal controversy in the appointment of this Jīyar. His predecessor, the 35th Jīyar (Kaḷathūr Svāmi) attained paramapada without appointing a successor. The 36th Jīyar was appointed by the disciples of the Ahobila Maṭha who were gathered at Tiruvaḷḷūr. Some of the disciples who were located outside Tiruvaḷḷūr desired to install the well-known pandit of Coladeśa, Śrī Saṭṭarka Vācaspati Kurucci Raṅgācārya Svāmī and hence filed a case in the High Court. However the judgement was in favour of Parandūr Svāmī and the problem was legally resolved. The related case and the ensuing judgement are recorded in the corresponding Law Journal. See Ācārya vaibhavam, p. 353. ↩︎

  162. It is stated that when this Jīyar attained paramapada Śrīmuṣṇam Śrī Svarṇam Kṛṣṇamācārya Svāmī is believed to have said that Śrībhāṣyam itself is ‘dead and gone’. See Ācārya vaibhavam, p. 353. [] divyadeśas Śrīperumbūdūr, Kāñcī, Madurāntakam, Tiruahindrapuram, Kumbhakoṇam, Pullambūtaṅguḍi, Ādanūr, and Śrīraṅgam. He was respected for his depth of knowledge in Tarka śāstra, Vedānta śāstra and Śrībhāṣya. ↩︎

Later, he proceeded to Śrī Nṛsiṁhapuram where he took ill suddenly and attained paramapada in the year Vikārī in the month of Mārgaḻi on pūrṇimā tithi (16.12.1899). This Svāmī did not nominate his successor and hence there was an interregnum for about five and a half years between 1899-1905. The Jīyar’s Bṛndāvanam is at Śrī Nṛsiṁhapuram.
